Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Six Hanafi Responses to the Objection on 'لا صلاة إلا بطهور'
Analyzing the hadith using usul principles of exception with six responses to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six Hanafi responses to the objection that the hadith implies absolute validity, and explain why the fifth response (the jumhur position) is preferred.

Core Idea
Exception from negation indicates conditionality, not automatic validity; the apparent generality is specialized by decisive proofs.

The hadith 'لا صلاة إلا بطهور' is often cited to prove purification is essential for prayer. But an opponent might argue that the exception from negation means any prayer with purification is valid, regardless of other conditions. This passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut presents six Hanafi responses to this objection, using tools of Usul al-Fiqh such as takhsis, analysis of exception structures, and recognition of customary usage. Understanding these responses deepens your ability to balance linguistic appearances with established rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and the Hanafi position
The opponent claims that exception from negation establishes the opposite ruling, so 'لا صلاة إلا بطهور' means any prayer with purification is valid. The Hanafis hold it only indicates purification is a condition, but other conditions are also necessary.
This sets up the dispute.
2
Examine the first two responses: Takhsis and refutation of discontinuity
The first response argues that the apparent generality is specified (mukhassis) by decisive proofs requiring other conditions. The second response refutes al-Amidi's claim of discontinuity by showing the exception is mufaragh (empty exception) and thus muttasil.
These responses address the literal structure.
3
Analyze responses three and four: Exaggeration and re-estimation
The third response interprets the hadith as hyperbole, but this is rejected as contrary to usul principles. The fourth response re-estimates the predicate to achieve consistency, but is critiqued for conflating sensory and shar'i attainment.
These show unsuccessful attempts.
4
Focus on the fifth response (the jumhur position)
The well-known response from the majority holds that the hadith establishes purification as a condition in general, but subject to other conditions. The objection that this makes the exception hesitant is refuted by noting that affirmation is not universal in every instance.
This is the relied-upon answer.
5
Understand the sixth response and the conclusion
The sixth response argues that the construction indicates conditionality only – the excepted-from is absent when the excepted is absent, but existence with the excepted is not affirmed textually. The author notes this concedes too much, and the fifth response remains the strongest.
Final evaluation: the fifth response is the most accepted.

لو كان في المستثنى حكم اللزم من لا صلاة إلا بطهور صحتها بمجرد الطهور) لإفادة الاستثناء مخالف الصدر (وهو باطل اتفاقاً فإن الصلاة مع فقدان شروط أخرى من الستر ونحوه، وإن كانت مع الطهارة باطلة قطعاً، وما في بعض شروح المنهاج من أن الحديث المذكور غير صحيح غير واف فإنه وإن لم تكن الألفاظ صحيحة، لكن الحديث بلفظ : «لا يقبل الله الصلاة إلا بطهور صحيح بل ادعى السيوطي ,تواتره وقد ذكر في رسالة مفردة أسانيد من المقارنة ولا مقارنة ههنا ولا احتمال للنسخ ههنا وهو غير واف، فإن اشتراط الشروط الأخر من ضروريات الدين وكان متقدماً عليه فيصلح مخصصاً، وإنما لا يصلح للتخصيص ما يظهر بعد ورود العام فافهم (و) يجاب (ثانياً كما قال الآمدي إنه منقطع فلا إخراج) فيه لشيء من أفراد الصلاة بل فيه حكم (آخر) من ثبوت الصحة مع الطهارة ولو في بعض الأحيان (ويدفع) هذا الجواب بأنه (مفرغ) لأن المعنى لا صلاة حاصلة ملتصقة بشيء إلا ملتصقة بطهور (وكل مفرغ متصل كما تقرر في النحو، وقد يقال : كونه مفرغاً غير متعين، إذ يجوز أن يكون التقدير هكذا : لا صلاة موجودة إلا صلاة بطهور , فالمستثنى منه هو الصلاة، فالوجه في الدفع أن يقال :أولاً : إن الانقطاع يفيد عدم صحة الصلاة عموماً، لكن قد تكون مقرونة بطهارة، وثانياً : إن الاتصال ممكن بل متبادر وظاهر، فلا يعدل إلى الانقطاع الذي يصار إليه بضرورة شديدة (و) يجاب (ثالثاً كما في «المنهاج بحمله على (المبالغة في اشتراط الطهارة (كأنه لا شرط للصحة غيرها) فلا يلزم الصحة مع فقدان سائر الشروط ولا يخفى أنه أي الحمل على المبالغة (خلاف الأصل سيما في الشرع فلا يصار إليه، كيف ولو فتح هذا الباب لما ثبت حكم أصلاً (و) يجاب (رابعاً) كما في المختصر أن قدر خبر مستثنى منه وقيل (لا صلاة صلاة إلا صلاة بطهور (اطرد الكل فإن كل صلاة بطهور ولو مع فقدان سائر الشروط (صلاة حاصلة (قطعاً فلا ,استحالة، وإن شئت جعلت الاستثناء عن الأحوال والمعنى لا صلاة حاصلة بحال إلا مقترنة بالطهارة وهو أوفق بكلامه، فإنه قال الإشكال في المستثنى منه فإنه يفيد عدم اتصاف الصلاة بحال غير الاقتران بالطهارة كما في ما زيد إلا قائماً (وليس) هذا الجواب (بشيء) لأنه إن أراد الحصول الشرعي، فالاطراد باطل لأن الحصول الشرعي غير مطرد لانتفاء سائر الشرائط) في بعض الصور ولا يوجد الشيء مع فقدان الشرائط، وإن أراد الحصول الحسي ففيه ما قال والحسي غير مراد بدليل الاستثناء) فإن الصلاة بدون الطهارة صلاة حسية، ولو قيل : إن الصلاة بدون سائر الشروط ليست صلاة حقيقة فيطرد الحصول الشرعي المقرونة بالطهارة قلت فعلى هذا كل صلاة صحيحة، لأن الصلاة بدون الطهارة ليست صلاة حقيقة، فيضيع الاستثناء حينئذ (و) يجاب (خامساً كما هو المشهور عن الجمهور أنه يفيد ثبوتها الطهور في الجملة ولو موقوفاً على شروط أخرى وذلك إذا تحقق سائر الشروط) المعتبرة في الصحة (ورد) هذا الجواب بأنه يجب) في الاستثناء من النفي أن يكون إثباتاً البتة لا أن يكون متردداً بين النفي والإثبات وههنا كذلك، فإن الحصول متردد بين أن يقع إذا تحقق سائر الشروط وبين أن لا يقع إذا لم يتحقق فتأمل) فإن الرد ليس بشيء، لأن مقصود المجيب أن الاستثناء من النفي إثبات لا أنه إثبات لكل فرد عموماً، وفي كل حين عموماً، فالمعنى أنه لا صلاة في حال من الأحوال أصلا إلا في حال الطهارة في الجملة قطعاً، وهذا لا تردد فيه أصلاً، وقد يردّ بأن المعنى لا صلاة صحيحة إلا صلاة بطهور فالنكرة موصوفة في الإثبات فيعم فيلزم صحة كل صلاة بطهور ولو مع فقدان سائر الشروط ولا جواب بعد تسليم هذا التقدير إلا بالرجوع إلى الأول من التزام التخصيص (و) يجاب سادساً بأن مثل هذا الكلام متعارف في إفادة الاشتراط) أي اشتراط المستثنى منه بالمستثنى (والتوقف) للمستثنى منه عليه (فيدل على انعدام المستثنى منه عند عدم المستثنى لأن فوات الشرط يوجب فوات المشروط، فالحديث إنما يدل على اشتراط الصلاة بالطهارة وعدمها بعدمها (أما أنه) أي المستثنى منه (يوجد معه أي مع المستثنى في الجملة فلا دلالة للفظ عليه فلا يدل الحديث على وجود مع الطهارة في الجملة وفيه ما فيه فإن فيه تسليم عدم الحكم في المستثنى، وكونه مثل المسكوت وهو مدعي الخصم وقد يوجه بعض الأجوبة بإرجاعها إلى المشهور، وهو الوجه الخامس فعليك بالتأويل.
The third article on linguistic principles: Existence is negation and from negation is existence, for this is considered from the excepted-from, meaning it is not a speech about everything that the excepted-from encompasses, but only about the remainder. (As for restricting to the ruling of the first part only, there is no text about it, rather it is silent about it, unlike that transmission, for it is textually established on the contrary ruling of the excepted such that it does not accept interpretation. And perhaps it is answered that the meaning is that he explicitly spoke about the remainder, and this does not negate that it implies a ruling contrary to the first part regarding the excepted. And from here, i.e., because this is the ruling of the excepted-from, it is known that what was said in the marginalia of Mirzajan on the commentary of al-Mukhtasar is repelled: that the statement of two contradictory rulings in the excepted-from and the excepted is not tenable with the choice that the attribution is after extraction. The face of repulsion is that this is the case of the excepted-from, for the attribution to it is after extraction, and this does not negate its conveying the contrary ruling in the excepted. So reflect. And by this is also repelled in al-Tawdih that what is more appropriate to this madhhab is that the excepted does not indicate the contrary ruling. (And) they said (secondly): If there were in the excepted a ruling requiring that from 'No prayer except with purification' its validity follows from mere purification, because exception indicates the contrary of the first part. And this is invalid by agreement, for prayer with the absence of other conditions like covering and the like, even if with purification, is absolutely invalid. And what is in some commentaries of al-Minhaj that the mentioned hadith is not authentic is insufficient, for even if the words are not authentic, the hadith with the wording 'Allah does not accept prayer except with purification' is authentic, indeed al-Suyuti claimed its tawatur, and he mentioned in a separate treatise numerous chains for it, so understand. (And it is answered) firstly, as I say: (The invalidity in some cases despite the presence of purification due to the opposition of a decisive proof that indicates the requirement of another matter like facing the qibla, covering, and other than that) does not harm our claim, for it is a specification (mukhassis) of the generality of the ruling of exception. It would only harm if we claimed absolute rulings and non-acceptance of specification, rather the claim is of apparentness even if it accepts specification. The upshot is that it is apparent in the establishment of validity with the absence of all other conditions were it not for the decisive opposing evidence. So understand. And it may be said: The mukhassis must be concomitant, and there is no concomitance here, nor is there a possibility of abrogation here, and that is insufficient, for the requirement of other conditions is from the necessities of the religion and was prior to it, so it qualifies as a mukhassis. Only what appears after the arrival of the general is not suitable for specification. So understand. (And) it is answered (secondly) as al-Amidi said: It is munqati' (discontinuous), so there is no extraction of any individuals of prayer in it, rather there is another ruling of establishing validity with purification, even if in some instances. (And) this answer is repelled because it is mufaragh (empty exception), because the meaning is 'No prayer attained attached to something except attached to purification'. And every mufaragh is connected (muttasil) as established in grammar. And it may be said: Its being mufaragh is not determinate, for it is possible that the estimation is like this: 'No existing prayer except a prayer with purification', so the excepted-from is prayer. So the face of repulsion is to say: Firstly, that discontinuity (inqita') indicates the general invalidity of prayer, but it may be accompanied by purification. Secondly, that continuity (ittisal) is possible, indeed obvious and apparent, so one should not turn to discontinuity which is resorted to only by extreme necessity. (And) it is answered (thirdly) as in al-Minhaj by interpreting it as exaggeration in requiring purification, as if there is no condition for validity other than it, so validity does not follow with the absence of other conditions. And it is not hidden that this interpretation (i.e., exaggeration) is contrary to the principle, especially in the Sharia, so it should not be resorted to. How so? If this door were opened, no ruling would ever be established. (And) it is answered (fourthly) as in al-Mukhtasar: that the predicate is estimated as an excepted-from, and it was said: 'No prayer for prayer, prayer except a prayer with purification.' Then everything is consistent, for every prayer with purification, even with the absence of other conditions, is an attained prayer absolutely, so there is no impossibility. And if you wish, you make the exception about states, and the meaning is: 'No prayer attained in any state except accompanied by purification,' and that is more in accordance with his statement, for he said: The problem is in the excepted-from, for it indicates that prayer is not qualified by any state other than being accompanied by purification, like in 'Zayd is not except standing.' (And) this answer is (nothing), because if he means shar'i attainment, then consistency is false because shar'i attainment is not consistent due to the absence of other conditions in some cases, and a thing does not exist with the absence of conditions. And if he means sensory attainment, then there is what he said, and sensory is not intended as indicated by the exception, for prayer without purification is sensory prayer. And if it is said: 'Prayer without other conditions is not truly prayer,' then shar'i attainment of the one accompanied by purification is consistent. I say: On this, every prayer is valid, because prayer without purification is not truly prayer, so the exception is then wasted. (And) it is answered (fifthly) as is well-known from the majority: that it indicates the establishment of purification absolutely, even if dependent on other conditions, and that is when the other conditions considered for validity are present. (And) this answer was refuted by saying that it is necessary for exception from negation to be affirmation absolutely, not to be hesitant between negation and affirmation, and here it is like that, for attainment is hesitant between occurring if other conditions are present and not occurring if they are not present. So reflect. But the refutation is nothing, because the intention of the answerer is that exception from negation is affirmation, not that it is affirmation for every individual universally and in every time universally. So the meaning is that there is no prayer in any case absolutely except in the case of purification absolutely, and this has no hesitation at all. And it may be refuted by saying that the meaning is 'No valid prayer except a prayer with purification,' so the indefinite noun (nakira) is qualified in affirmation and is general, so it would require the validity of every prayer with purification even with the absence of other conditions, and there is no answer after accepting this estimation except by resorting to the first answer of adhering to specification (takhsis). (And) it is answered (sixthly) that such speech is customary in indicating the conditionality of the excepted-from by the excepted and its dependence on it, so it indicates the cessation of the excepted-from in the absence of the excepted because the loss of condition necessitates the loss of the conditioned. So the hadith only indicates the conditionality of prayer by purification and its absence by its absence. As for the excepted-from existing with the excepted absolutely, there is no indication in the wording, so the hadith does not indicate existence with purification absolutely. And there is something in this, for it involves conceding the absence of ruling in the excepted, and its being like the unmentioned, which is the claim of the opponent. And some of the answers may be directed by returning them to the well-known, which is the fifth way. So you are to reflect.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of the linguistic principle of exception (istithna) as applied to the hadith 'لا صلاة إلا بطهور' (No prayer except with purification). The author (the commentator on Musallam al-Thubut) is expanding on the matn's discussion of whether exception from negation automatically establishes the opposite ruling (i.e., that any prayer with purification is valid). The passage presents six responses to objections against the Hanafi position that the hadith does not imply automatic validity but rather conditionality. The first response argues that the hadith's apparent generality can be specialized (takhsis) by other necessary conditions like facing qibla and covering. The second response, attributed to al-Amidi, claims the exception is discontinuous (munqati), but this is refuted by showing it is mufaragh (empty exception) which is connected. The third response interprets the hadith as exaggeration, which is rejected as contrary to usul. The fourth response suggests a different estimation of the predicate to achieve consistency, but is critiqued. The fifth response, attributed to the majority (jumhur), holds that the hadith establishes purification as a condition in general but subject to other conditions; it is defended against the objection of hesitation. The sixth response states that the construction indicates conditionality only, not existence of the excepted-from with the excepted. The author concludes by noting that the fifth response is the most well-known and that other answers may be referred back to it. The passage reflects the Hanafi usul that exception from negation indicates a conditional relationship, not unqualified validity, and can be subject to specification by other decisive proofs.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception: a linguistic construction that excludes something from a general statement. In this passage, exception from negation is discussed.
مستثنى منه (mustathna minhu): The excepted-from: the main category from which the exception is made. In 'لا صلاة إلا بطهور', the excepted-from is 'prayer' (الصلاة).
مستثنى (mustathna): The excepted: the item that is excluded from the general rule. In the example, it is 'prayer with purification'.
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification: limiting a general ('amm) ruling to a subset of its individuals. Used here to argue that the hadith's general appearance is specified by other conditions.
انقطاع (inqita'): Discontinuity: a type of exception where the excepted is not actually a part of the excepted-from. Al-Amidi claimed the hadith is munqati'.
مفرغ (mufaragh): Empty exception: an exception where the excepted-from is omitted from the first part of the sentence. The text argues that the hadith is mufaragh and therefore muttasil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul analysis in the passage, why does the hadith 'لا صلاة إلا بطهور' not imply that any prayer with purification is valid?
A) Because the hadith is inauthentic.
B) Because the exception is discontinuous (munqati).
C) Because decisive proofs requiring other conditions (like facing qibla) specify the apparent generality.
D) Because the hadith is meant as exaggeration only.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'لا صلاة إلا بطهور' establishes purification as a necessary condition for prayer, but the validity of prayer also depends on other conditions established by decisive proofs; the exception indicates conditionality, not automatic validity.

Exception from Negation Indicates the Opposite Ruling
Explaining the Hanafi rule that exception from negation indicates the opposite ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that exception from negation indicates the opposite ruling and apply it to a simple legal text.

Core Idea
Exception from negation positively affirms the opposite ruling for the excepted item.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational principle of usul al-fiqh: how exception (istithna') functions after a negative statement. The Hanafi school, in agreement with the majority, holds that the excepted item takes the opposite ruling of the negation. This rule is crucial for understanding many legal texts. We'll explore the reasoning that likens exception to a limit (ghaya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
The passage states that exception from negation indicates the opposite ruling in the excepted item. This applies when a general negation is followed by an exception using 'illa' (except).
The rule is specific to negation, not affirmative statements.
2
Understand the analogy to ghaya (limit)
Exception is compared to a limit (ghaya) — just as a limit marks the end of one thing and the beginning of another, exception marks the boundary of the negation and implies the opposite for the excepted case.
This analogy is the key to understanding the logic behind the rule.
3
Apply the rule to an example
Consider the statement 'No prayer is valid without purity' (لا صلاة إلا بطهور). The exception 'except with purity' indicates that prayer with purity is valid — the opposite of the general negation.
This example shows the practical legal consequence.
4
Note the scholarly agreement
The Hanafi achieving scholars agreed with the majority of usul scholars on this rule, indicating its wide acceptance despite minor differences in phrasing.
This rule is not unique to Hanafis but is highlighted here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة (الثانية : الحنفية المحققون الذين وافقوا الجمهور) في أن الاستثناء يفيد الحكم المخالف في المستثنى قالوا : الحكم الذي بعد إلا إشارة لأنه أي الاستثناء (بمنزلة الغاية)
The second benefit: The Hanafi achieving scholars who agreed with the majority (say) that exception indicates the opposite ruling in the excepted item. They said: The ruling after 'illa' is an indication because exception is like a limit.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents a key usul rule from the Hanafi school regarding exception (istithna'). The rule is that when an exception is made from a negative statement, it indicates that the opposite ruling applies to the excepted item. For example, from 'No prayer is valid without purity', the exception 'except with purity' indicates that prayer with purity is valid. The scholars liken exception to a limit (ghaya), meaning it marks the boundary of the negation. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul for understanding exceptive clauses.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exception; a grammatical construction that excludes some items from a general statement.
الغاية (al-ghaya): Limit; the point at which exception terminates the general ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars mentioned, what does exception (istithna') from a negation indicate?
A) It indicates the same ruling as the rest
B) It indicates the opposite ruling in the excepted item
C) It indicates uncertainty
D) It indicates no ruling

Key Takeaway
Exception (istithna') after a negation positively indicates the opposite ruling for the excepted item, functioning like a limit that marks the boundary.

Nature of Istithna: Isharah vs Ibarah
Refines understanding of istithna as isharah or ibarah based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an istithna (exception) functions as isharah (implicit indication) or ibarah (explicit expression) and apply the Hanafi usul principle that the speaker's intention determines the category.

Core Idea
Istithna is implicitly indicative of a contrary ruling unless the speaker explicitly intends both affirmation and negation.

In this lesson, we delve into the nuanced nature of istithna (exception) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The passage clarifies that whether an exception serves as an implicit indication (isharah) or an explicit expression (ibarah) depends on the speaker's primary intention. This distinction is crucial for deriving rulings from texts containing exceptions, as it affects how we interpret the scope of the general term. We will explore examples from the Kalimah al-Tawhid and everyday legal statements to understand this classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of istithna
Recognize that istithna can be isharah (implicit indication) when the primary intent is the remainder, and ibarah (explicit expression) when both affirmation and negation are equally intended.
2
Analyze the example 'علي عشرة إلا ثلاثة'
Determine that the primary intent is to affirm the remainder (seven), so the negation of the excluded part is subsidiary, making it isharah.
The negation of the excess follows subsidiarily.
3
Analyze the example of the Kalimah al-Tawhid
In 'لا إله إلا الله', both affirmation (Allah) and negation (no god) are equally intended, making it ibarah.
The passage notes a possible interpretation that only negation is intended, but the primary example treats both as intended.
4
Consider the special case of istithna mufarrag
In absolute exception like 'ما أنت إلا حر', the second part (free) may be intended primarily, while the first part is subsidiary, so it can be isharah for the first part.
5
Evaluate the refutation of Sadr al-Shari'ah
Understand the author's rebuttal that the longer expression (mentioning the whole then excluding) serves an indicative purpose, defending that istithna can be isharah even when the composite whole indicates.
The refutation is that the very structure of exception implies a contrary ruling.

فإنها لانتهاء حكم الصدر ، وتفيد عدم دخول ما بعده فيما قبله كذلك الاستثناء وغاية الوجود عدم وبالعكس فلزم فيه الحكم المخالف (إلا) أن الصدر ثابت قصداً وهذا لا بل تبعاً، فيكون إشارة (والأوجه) على ما في «التحرير» أن هذا ليس على الإطلاق بل (أنه إشارة مرة) إن لم يكن مقصوداً (نحو : علي عشرة إلا ثلاثة ، لأن المقصود منه (سبعة) أي الإقرار به، وأما نفي ما زاد فيلزم تبعاً (و) أنه (عبارة) ومقصود مرة أخرى ككلمة التوحيد، فإن الإثبات والنفي) المفهومين فيها (كلاهما مقصودان) وقد يقال : لا قصد إلا إلى النفي، لأن المخاطب غير لكنه مشرك، فالمقصود منهما رد زعمه، واكتفى في الإثبات بمجرد الإشارة، وهذا محتمل غير ضار لأصل المقصود، إذ لا يزيد على المناقشة في المثال فتأمل (بل قد يقصد الثاني) بالذات (فقط) دون الأول إلا تبعاً في الاستثناء المفرّغ (نحو : ما أنت إلا حر فافهم وتحقيق كلامهم قدس أسرارهم أنك قد عرفت أن الألفاظ في الاستثناء مستعملة في معانيها ويحصل من المركب مفهوم تقييدي يحكم عليه وعرفت أنه يقيد المستثنى منه بإخراج البعض فيحصل مقيد هو المستثنى منه المنقوص منه البعض، ويعبر به عن الباقي، وفي هذا التعبير الأطول بذكر الكل، ثم إخراج البعض إشارة إلى أن المخرج مخالف للصدر في الحكم، وهذه النكتة في الإطناب واختيار طريق أطول فحينئذ اندفع ما قال صدر الشريعة إن هذا إنما يصح باختيار القول الثاني هو أن يذكر الكل ويحكم على البعض، وأما على اختيار أن المجموع المركب هو الدال فالتخصيص بمفهوم اللقب ولا يكون إشارة، ووجه الدفع ظاهر فتأمل جداً، ولعل من قال إنه لا حكم فيه لغة إنما يفهم عرفاً ،مراده، هذا يعني ليس اللفظ موضوعاً لإفادة الحكم التام بالذات، بل إنما هو قيد يستفاد الحكم ضمناً وإشارة، ويؤيده ما اتفقوا عليه أن المفرد لا يدل على جملة، ثم هذا الذي ذكر هو الأصل في الاستثناء، وقد يعدل عنه فتقصد هذه الإشارة في خصوص التركيب بالذات، فلا إشكال عليهم قدس أسرارهم إلا من جهة عدم التدبر في كلامهم.
For it is for the termination of the ruling of the first part, and it indicates the non-inclusion of what comes after in what comes before, similarly the exception and the ultimate end of existence is non-existence and vice versa, so a contradictory ruling is necessary for it. (Except) that the first part is established intentionally, and this [the exception] is only subsidiary, so it is an indication. (And the most appropriate) according to what is in al-Tahrir is that this is not absolute, but (that it is an indication sometimes) if it is not intended (like: I have ten except three, because the intended is seven, i.e., the acknowledgment of it. As for the negation of the excess, it follows subsidiarily). And (that) it is (an expression) and intended at other times like the word of tawhid, for the affirmation and negation understood in it are both intended. And it may be said: there is no intention except for negation, because the addressee is other but he is a polytheist, so the intended from them is to refute his claim, and he is satisfied in affirmation with mere indication. And this is probable and not harmful to the original intent, since it does not exceed quibbling in the example, so reflect. (Rather the second may be intended) essentially (only) without the first except subsidiarily in the absolute exception (like: You are nothing but free, so understand. And the verification of their words, may their secrets be sanctified, is that you have known that the words in exception are used in their meanings and from the composite a restrictive concept emerges that is judged upon, and you have known that it restricts the excepted from by removing some part, so a restricted is obtained which is the excepted from diminished by that part, and by it the remainder is expressed. And in this longer expression of mentioning the whole then removing some part is an indication that the removed is contrary to the first part in ruling. And this subtlety in elaboration and choosing a longer path then it repels what Sadr al-Shari'ah said: that this is only valid by choosing the second opinion that the whole is mentioned and a part is judged. But according to the choice that the composite whole is what indicates, then specification is by the concept of the title and it is not an indication. And the repelling is apparent, so reflect greatly. And perhaps he who said that there is no ruling in it linguistically, it is understood customarily - his intent is this: that the word is not laid down for expressing the complete ruling essentially, but rather it is a restriction through which the ruling is understood implicitly and by indication. And it is supported by their agreement that the singular does not indicate a sentence. Then this that was mentioned is the principle in exception, and sometimes one deviates from it so this indication is intended in the particular construction essentially, so there is no difficulty on them, may their secrets be sanctified, except due to lack of reflection in their words.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from the sharh of Musallam al-Thubut, refining the nature of istithna (exception) in usul al-fiqh. The author explains that whether an exception functions as isharah (implicit indication) or ibarah (explicit expression) depends on the speaker's primary intention. He begins by noting that the exception indicates a ruling contradictory to the first part (mustathna minhu), and it can be isharah when the main intent is the affirmation of the remainder, like saying 'I have ten except three' where the focus is on seven granted. Conversely, it can be ibarah when both affirmation and negation are equally intended, as in the Kalimah al-Tawhid. The discussion specifically addresses istithna mufarrag (absolute exception) where the second part may be intended essentially, e.g., 'You are nothing but free.' The author then refutes a claim by Sadr al-Shari'ah by explaining that the longer expression of mentioning the whole and then excluding a part serves an indicative purpose, highlighting the contrast in ruling. The analysis clarifies that the ruling is derived not from the individual words but from the composite structure, and that the exception's function is context-dependent. The Hanafi position here is that the exception is fundamentally an implicit indication, though it can be used explicitly when the speaker chooses.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna): A linguistic operator that excludes some of what is included in the general term, indicating a ruling contrary to the first part.
إشارة (isharah): An implicit indication of the ruling not directly expressed but understood contextually.
عبارة (ibarah): An explicit statement directly expressing the intended ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does istithna function as isharah (indication)?
A) When both parts are intended equally, like in the Kalimah al-Tawhid.
B) When the primary intent is the remainder and the negation of what is excluded is subsidiary.
C) When the exception is muqayyad (restricted).
D) When it is used in a mufarrag (absolute) construction.

Key Takeaway
The classification of istithna as isharah or ibarah depends on the speaker's intention, and Hanafi usul emphasizes that it is fundamentally implicit unless the speaker explicitly intends both affirmation and negation equally.

The Dispute Over the Excluded Item: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explains the dispute over the effect of exception on the excluded item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the excluded item carries no ruling and compare it with the Shafi'i view, and apply the linguistic principle of 'closest kind' to determine the scope of an exception.

Core Idea
The excluded item is like the unmentioned: no ruling applies to it, only to the remainder.

This passage presents the well-known dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether an exception (istithna) assigns a ruling to the excluded item (mustathna). The Hanafis argue that the excluded item is like the unmentioned (maskut), carrying no ruling, while the Shafi'is disagree. Understanding this disagreement is crucial for deriving fiqh rulings from texts that contain exceptions, such as the hadith on food exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What exactly is the point of disagreement? The text states that Hanafis hold 'no ruling on the excluded item' while Shafi'is hold a ruling exists. Determine the core dispute.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafis, the excluded item (e.g., 'equal' in 'except equal') has no ruling; it is like an unmentioned topic. The ruling applies only to the remainder after the exception.
Mustathna = maskut anhu
3
Understand the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'is consider that the exception itself imposes a ruling on the excluded item. In the case of food, they hold that the exception 'except equal' means equality is permitted, but all other cases (including items not measured by volume) remain prohibited.
4
Analyze the linguistic basis
The Hanafis cite linguistic intuition (tabaddur) and Imam Muhammad's example about 'no one in the house except Zaid' to show that the exception's scope is determined by the closest kind (naw') to the excluded item, not the genus (jins).
Closest kind = aqrab al-naw'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: For items not measured by volume (e.g., weighed items like gold), Hanafis permit sale if equal in weight, while Shafi'is prohibit it unless equal in both weight and volume.

إلا زيد، وعلى هذا قال الإمام محمد : إن كان في الدار إلا زيد فعبدي حر، إن المستثنى منه بنو آدم، ولو قال : إلاّ حمار كان المستثنى منه الحيوان ولو قال إلا متاع كان المستثنى منه كل شيء، فعلم أن المستثنى Sale of what... The third article on linguistic principles: a handful of food (for example, two handfuls) because it falls under the generality of the text. And according to the Hanafis: there is no ruling on the excluded item (mustathna), which is equality, because it is like the unmentioned (maskut) according to them; rather, the ruling is on the remainder after the exception—which is superiority (tafadul) in reality or in semblance like estimation (mujazafah), so sale is prohibited in these two only. And they are in items measured by volume (kayli) by customary measure because only equality is considered in it. For example, if wheat is sold with its genus equal in volume but differing in weight, it is permissible. Similarly, weighed items like gold, if sold with its genus equal in weight but not in volume, and the opposite is not permissible in either. So what does not fall under it is not mentioned in the beginning (sadr), and the original principle is permissibility, so it remains permissible. And there is an apparent objection: after assuming the ruling on equality, the purpose is achieved—it is a solution that does not fall under volume either. For affirmation and negation only occur in what falls under volume because it is the thing from which exception is made. And what is outside volume remains outside the ruling of the text. And there is no difference between the two schools except that the permissibility in equality when the ruling is on the excluded item is stated by the explicit words (mantuq), and when it is not, it is by the original principle. Then his speech has another objection: that the Shafi'is only argued by the ruling on the thing from which exception is made, not by the ruling on the excluded item. So their purpose is established whether the ruling is on it or not. For the essence of their proof is that he excepted the state of equality in the standard, so all other states remain prohibited, including the sale of what does not enter the standard due to lack of equality. And a third objection: that if the basis of the dispute were what was mentioned, then Imam Fakhr al-Islam and his likes would say prohibition, because they assert a ruling on the excluded item. Then these problems only apply to whoever interpreted his speech in this manner, and it does not correspond to his speech. Rather, they arise from poor understanding and lack of reflection. His intention was not to base the dispute on it; rather, he transmitted the Shafi'i school that exception repels the ruling by opposition (mu'aradah) like specification (takhsis). Then he clarified this in the hadith which is one of its particulars and other examples. His purpose was not to base the dispute on it; rather, the purpose was to give examples of exception. His words: thus according to us the estimation of a man's saying: 'I owe Zaid one thousand dirhams except one hundred' is that I owe Zaid nine hundred, and according to Shafi'i, 'except one hundred' means they are not upon me. The explanation is that he made the verse ﴾إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوا﴿ [An-Nur:5] to mean: except those who repent, do not flog them and accept their testimonies, and those are the righteous, not sinners. Similarly, he said regarding the Prophet's ﷺ saying: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal', so the beginning of the statement remained general in little and much, because the exception opposed it in measured items specifically, and the specificity of the proof of opposition does not extend beyond that, like the proof of specificity in a general statement. End of his noble words. Look with the eye of fairness: is there any trace of basing the dispute in the mentioned branch on it? The evidence is that he said in response to the Shafi'is regarding their statement in the section on qiyas: the estimated thing from which exception is made is the states of measurement (ahwal kayliyyah) as the author chooses by his saying: 'the most appropriate (al-awjah)'. And if the basis were this dispute, it would suffice him the trouble of the answer. 'The most appropriate' according to what is in at-Tahrir, taken from the words of this great scholar Imam Fakhr al-Islam in the chapter on qiyas, is that its basis is consideration of the type (naw') of the empty (mufarraq) exception and its estimation, or its genus. According to the Hanafis, the first is considered: they estimated the type of equality, which is the state of measurement (hal kayliyyah) that includes equality, superiority, and estimation; so what does not fall under measurement remains outside the ruling of prohibition. The Shafi'is estimated the second, i.e., its genus, which is the absolute state (mutlaq al-hal) that includes estimation by volume, and its non-inclusion enters prohibition. And the preponderant is the first, i.e., estimation of the type, because what is immediately understood (mutabadir) from 'who is in the house except Zaid' is that there is no human in the house except Zaid, not 'no animal except Zaid'. On this, Imam Muhammad said: If someone says 'there is no one in the house except Zaid, and my slave is free', the thing from which exception is made is human beings; if he said 'except a donkey', the thing from which exception is made is animals; if he said 'except furniture', the thing from which exception is made is everything. So it is known that the thing from which exception is made is what is closest to the excluded item. Perhaps this is apparent to anyone with minimal induction and reflection in speech. This, and Allah knows the reality of the situation.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an extended usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the effect of an exception (istithna) on the excluded item (mustathna). The author first states the Hanafi position: the excluded item carries no ruling; it is like the unmentioned (maskut). The ruling applies only to the remainder (mustathna minhu). This is applied to the hadith prohibiting unequal exchange of food except when equal. The Hanafis interpret the exception as specifying the state of equality (musawa) in the type (naw') of measurement, so only exchanges of items measured by volume (kayli) are covered; items not so measured are outside the ruling and thus permissible by default (ibahah). The Shafi'is, by contrast, consider the genus (jins) of the exception, so all exchanges fall under the prohibition unless equal. The author then defends the earlier scholar (likely al-Pazdawi or similar) against criticisms, clarifying that the dispute is not about the effect on the excluded item per se but about the proper interpretation of the exception in the hadith. He cites examples from the Quran and the hadith, and refers to Imam Muhammad's linguistic analysis to show that the exception's scope is determined by the closest kind (aqrab) to the excluded item. The preponderant view is the Hanafi one, as supported by linguistic intuition (tabaddur). The passage concludes with 'Allah knows best'.

Key Terms
المستثنى (al-mustathna): The excluded item in an exception; the item that is subtracted from the generality of the statement, e.g., 'except Zaid'.
المستثنى منه (al-mustathna minhu): The thing from which exception is made; the general category from which the excluded item is subtracted.
نوع (naw'): Type or species; a specific category within a genus, e.g., the state of equality in volume (hal kayliyyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what determines the scope of an exception?
A) The genus (jins) of the excluded item
B) The type (naw') that is closest to the excluded item
C) The literal wording of the exception
D) The context of the entire statement

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the excluded item carries no ruling; the exception's scope is determined by the closest type to the excluded item, as supported by linguistic intuition.

Applying the Exception in the Hadith on Food Exchange
Applies the exception dispute to the food exchange hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the exception in the hadith on food exchange specifies only the type (naw') of measurement by volume, and distinguish between items measured by volume and those measured by weight.

Core Idea
The exception 'except equal' in the food hadith only applies to items measured by volume; other items are permissible when equal in their own measure.

The Prophet's prohibition of selling food for food except equally is a key text in Islamic commercial law. The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ on its scope. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and usul principles that determine which items are covered by the exception, and the practical consequences for transactions involving weighed items like gold.

Guided Path
1
State the hadith and its apparent meaning
The hadith says: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' At face value, it prohibits unequal exchange of food, allowing only equal exchange. But what counts as 'food' and what counts as 'equal'?
2
Identify the Hanafi interpretation
Hanafis consider that the exception specifies the state of equality in the type of measurement that is customary for each food: for items measured by volume (kayl), equality in volume is required; for items measured by weight (wazn), equality in weight. This is because the exception applies to the type (naw') of measurement.
Al-naw' = the hal kayliyyah for kayli items, hal wazniyyah for wazni items
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i interpretation
Shafi'is consider the genus (jins) of measurement, so any food must be equal in whatever measure is customary, but they also require hand-to-hand transfer and sometimes include all food under the same prohibition.
4
Analyze the linguistic evidence
The Hanafi view relies on the linguistic principle that the exception's scope is the closest kind to the excluded item. Imam Muhammad's example of 'no one in the house except Zaid' shows that the mustathna minhu is human beings (the closest type), not all living creatures.
5
Apply to a concrete case
If a man sells wheat (a kayli item) for wheat equal in volume but different in weight, the sale is permissible according to Hanafis but prohibited according to Shafi'is. For gold (a wazn item), Hanafis allow if weights are equal even if volumes differ.

Sale of what... The third article on linguistic principles: a handful of food (for example, two handfuls) because it falls under the generality of the text. And according to the Hanafis: there is no ruling on the excluded item (mustathna), which is equality, because it is like the unmentioned (maskut) according to them; rather, the ruling is on the remainder after the exception—which is superiority (tafadul) in reality or in semblance like estimation (mujazafah), so sale is prohibited in these two only. And they are in items measured by volume (kayli) by customary measure because only equality is considered in it. For example, if wheat is sold with its genus equal in volume but differing in weight, it is permissible. Similarly, weighed items like gold, if sold with its genus equal in weight but not in volume, and the opposite is not permissible in either. So what does not fall under it is not mentioned in the beginning (sadr), and the original principle is permissibility, so it remains permissible. And there is an apparent objection: after assuming the ruling on equality, the purpose is achieved—it is a solution that does not fall under volume either. For affirmation and negation only occur in what falls under volume because it is the thing from which exception is made. And what is outside volume remains outside the ruling of the text. And there is no difference between the two schools except that the permissibility in equality when the ruling is on the excluded item is stated by the explicit words (mantuq), and when it is not, it is by the original principle. Then his speech has another objection: that the Shafi'is only argued by the ruling on the thing from which exception is made, not by the ruling on the excluded item. So their purpose is established whether the ruling is on it or not. For the essence of their proof is that he excepted the state of equality in the standard, so all other states remain prohibited, including the sale of what does not enter the standard due to lack of equality. And a third objection: that if the basis of the dispute were what was mentioned, then Imam Fakhr al-Islam and his likes would say prohibition, because they assert a ruling on the excluded item. Then these problems only apply to whoever interpreted his speech in this manner, and it does not correspond to his speech. Rather, they arise from poor understanding and lack of reflection. His intention was not to base the dispute on it; rather, he transmitted the Shafi'i school that exception repels the ruling by opposition (mu'aradah) like specification (takhsis). Then he clarified this in the hadith which is one of its particulars and other examples. His purpose was not to base the dispute on it; rather, the purpose was to give examples of exception. His words: thus according to us the estimation of a man's saying: 'I owe Zaid one thousand dirhams except one hundred' is that I owe Zaid nine hundred, and according to Shafi'i, 'except one hundred' means they are not upon me. The explanation is that he made the verse ﴾إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوا﴿ [An-Nur:5] to mean: except those who repent, do not flog them and accept their testimonies, and those are the righteous, not sinners. Similarly, he said regarding the Prophet's ﷺ saying: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal', so the beginning of the statement remained general in little and much, because the exception opposed it in measured items specifically, and the specificity of the proof of opposition does not extend beyond that, like the proof of specificity in a general statement. End of his noble words. Look with the eye of fairness: is there any trace of basing the dispute in the mentioned branch on it? The evidence is that he said in response to the Shafi'is regarding their statement in the section on qiyas: the estimated thing from which exception is made is the states of measurement (ahwal kayliyyah) as the author chooses by his saying: 'the most appropriate (al-awjah)'. And if the basis were this dispute, it would suffice him the trouble of the answer. 'The most appropriate' according to what is in at-Tahrir, taken from the words of this great scholar Imam Fakhr al-Islam in the chapter on qiyas, is that its basis is consideration of the type (naw') of the empty (mufarraq) exception and its estimation, or its genus. According to the Hanafis, the first is considered: they estimated the type of equality, which is the state of measurement (hal kayliyyah) that includes equality, superiority, and estimation; so what does not fall under measurement remains outside the ruling of prohibition. The Shafi'is estimated the second, i.e., its genus, which is the absolute state (mutlaq al-hal) that includes estimation by volume, and its non-inclusion enters prohibition. And the preponderant is the first, i.e., estimation of the type, because what is immediately understood (mutabadir) from 'who is in the house except Zaid' is that there is no human in the house except Zaid, not 'no animal except Zaid'. On this, Imam Muhammad said: If someone says 'there is no one in the house except Zaid, and my slave is free', the thing from which exception is made is human beings; if he said 'except a donkey', the thing from which exception is made is animals; if he said 'except furniture', the thing from which exception is made is everything. So it is known that the thing from which exception is made is what is closest to the excluded item. Perhaps this is apparent to anyone with minimal induction and reflection in speech. This, and Allah knows the reality of the situation.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an extended usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the effect of an exception (istithna) on the excluded item (mustathna). The author first states the Hanafi position: the excluded item carries no ruling; it is like the unmentioned (maskut). The ruling applies only to the remainder (mustathna minhu). This is applied to the hadith prohibiting unequal exchange of food except when equal. The Hanafis interpret the exception as specifying the state of equality (musawa) in the type (naw') of measurement, so only exchanges of items measured by volume (kayli) are covered; items not so measured are outside the ruling and thus permissible by default (ibahah). The Shafi'is, by contrast, consider the genus (jins) of the exception, so all exchanges fall under the prohibition unless equal. The author then defends the earlier scholar (likely al-Pazdawi or similar) against criticisms, clarifying that the dispute is not about the effect on the excluded item per se but about the proper interpretation of the exception in the hadith. He cites examples from the Quran and the hadith, and refers to Imam Muhammad's linguistic analysis to show that the exception's scope is determined by the closest kind (aqrab) to the excluded item. The preponderant view is the Hanafi one, as supported by linguistic intuition (tabaddur). The passage concludes with 'Allah knows best'.

Key Terms
المستثنى (al-mustathna): The excluded item in an exception; in the hadith, 'equal' is the mustathna.
المستثنى منه (al-mustathna minhu): The general category from which exception is made; in the hadith, it is the state of measurement (al-hal al-kayliyyah or al-wazniyyah).
نوع (naw'): Type or species; the specific category of measurement relevant to the item (e.g., volume for kayli).
جنس (jins): Genus; the broader category of measurement encompassing all types.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling gold for gold equal in weight but different in volume?
A) Prohibited because the volumes differ.
B) Permissible because gold is a weighed item and equality in weight suffices.
C) Permissible only if the transaction is hand-to-hand.
D) Prohibited because gold is not measured by volume.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of the exception in the food hadith limits the prohibition to the type of measure customary for each item, allowing more flexibility in trade.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Exception After Multiple Coordinate Clauses: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Usul dispute on the operation of istithna after coordinate clauses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the scope of istithna after coordinate clauses and apply the concept of zuhur to distinguish possibility from appearance.

Core Idea
The Hanafi rule: an exception after multiple clauses only excludes from the last clause—based on apparent meaning, not certainty.

When a speaker says 'Honor Banu Tamim and hire Mudar except Zaid', does the exception exclude Zaid from both commands or only from the last? This is a classical usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is. The disagreement is not about what is linguistically possible, but about which meaning is the apparent (zahir) one. In this lesson, we will unpack the positions, proofs, and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual context
Recognize that the passage discusses an exception (istithna) following multiple clauses joined by coordinating particles (waw, fa, thumma). The example 'Honor Banu Tamim and hire Mudar except Zaid' is the case under dispute.
The sentence is constructed to illustrate the difference in interpretation.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi school holds that the exception applies only to the last clause. Their basis (makhadh) is the apparent meaning (zuhur): the immediate linguistic intuition is that the exception attaches to the nearest clause.
They cite grammarians like Abu Ali al-Farisi in support.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its basis
The Shafi'i school holds that the exception applies to all clauses. Their basis is that the conjunction (waw) joins the clauses as a single compound command, so the exception should be understood as modifying the entire compound.
They rely on the generality of the exception tool and the joining function of waw.
4
Distinguish between possibility and appearance
Both schools agree that it is linguistically possible for the exception to return to the last, to all, or even to only some clauses. The dispute is solely about which meaning is the apparent (zahir) one, not which is possible.
No one claims the text is clear (nass) for a single interpretation.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In the example, the Hanafi view means Zaid is excluded only from hiring, so he must still be honored. The Shafi'i view means Zaid is excluded from both, so he is neither honored nor hired. This difference has real legal impact in wills, oaths, and testaments.
Other scholars (al-Baqillani, al-Ghazali) adopt suspension (waqf) but agree in outcome with Hanafis regarding the last clause.

مسألة الاستثناء بعد جمل متعاطفة بالواو ونحوه من الفاء وثم كما في «التحرير» يتعلق بالأخيرة) فقط عندنا كأبي علي الفارسي من (النحاة أي كما ذهب هو إليه (و) يتعلق بالكل) أي كل واحد عند الشافعية كابن مالك منهم قال في شرح المختصر والنزاع في الظهور) فعندنا ظاهر في تعلقه بالأخير وعندهم في التعلق بكل (لا) (الإمكان أي لا في إمكان التعلق فإنه ثبت عوده إلى الكل) أي كل واحد (و) ثبت عوده إلى ما عدا الأخيرة وإلى الأخيرة فقط وإلى ما عدا الأولى فقط فلا يتأتى من أحد دعوى النصوصية في واحد من الاحتمالات، وإنما يصلح للنزاع الظهور . من وجد اعلم أن الظهور في الأخيرة منصوص في شرح (البديع ويظهر من كلام الإمام النسفي رحمه الله تعالى وما قال الشيخ ابن الهمام أن الحنفية لم يصرحوا به، بل إنما صرحوا بالرجوع إلى الأخيرة، ويحتمل أن يكونوا متوقفين في الظهور، وأن ما نسب للشافعية أخذاً دليلهم، فشهادة على النفي لا بد من تصحيحه بالاستقراء البالغ وليس بل تصريح الأئمة خلاف ذلك كما عرفت وعلى التنزل فدلالة الدليل مع تفرع الفروع واحتمال العبارات بل ظهورها كافية في صحة النسبة فافهم وقال القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني (و) الإمام الهمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي بالوقف لعدم العلم بأنه حقيقة في أيهما في الأخيرة فقط أو في الكل (و) قال (المرتضى) من الروافض (بالاشتراك) فيهما (فيتوقف إلى ظهور القرينة، قال شارح المختصر (وهذان) القولان الوقف والاشتراك يوافقان لنا في الحكم لأنهما قاضيان بالتعلق بالأخيرة والوقف في غيرها إلى أن يقوم دليل (وإن خالفا نا (في المأخذ لأن مأخذهم في تعين الأخيرة التيقن به فإنه إن كان لها خاصة فظاهر، وإن كان للكل فلها أيضاً، ولا احتمال لكونه لما عداها من غير قرينة وكذا في الاشتراك، وأما عندنا فالمأخذ الظهور في الأخيرة وقال أبو الحسين المعتزلي إن ظهر الإضراب عن الجملة الأولى بأن يختلفا نوعاً من الإنشائية والخبرية والأمرية والنهيية (أو اسماً بأن يكون الاسم الصالح للاستثناء عنه مختلفاً (أو حكماً) بأن يكون حكمهما مختلفاً نحو أكرم بني تميم واستأجر مضر (و) الحال أنه (لا يكون في الثاني ضمير (الأوّل أي لا يكون في الكلام الثاني ضمير يرجع إلى الاسم المذكور في الأول
Issue: The exception after coordinate clauses joined by waw and similar particles like fa and thumma, as in at-Tahrir, pertains to the last only, according to us (Hanafis), similar to Abu Ali al-Farisi among the grammarians, i.e., as he held. And it pertains to all, i.e., each one, according to the Shafi'is, like Ibn Malik among them, as he said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar. The dispute is about the apparent meaning (zuhur). According to us, it is apparent that it pertains to the last; according to them, that it pertains to all. Not about possibility (imkan), for the possibility of returning to all is established; i.e., it is established that it can return to each one, and it is established that it can return to all except the last, and to the last only, and to all except the first only. So no one can claim textuality (nassiyyah) for any one of the probabilities. Only appearance (zuhur) is suitable for dispute. Whoever finds... Know that the appearance of the last is attested in Sharh al-Badi'. It appears from the statement of Imam al-Nasafi (may Allah have mercy on him) and what Sheikh Ibn al-Humam said that the Hanafis did not explicitly state it; rather, they explicitly stated the return to the last, and it is possible that they were undecided about the appearance. And what is attributed to the Shafi'is is taken from their evidence, so a testimony of negation must be corrected by thorough induction, and it is not an explicit statement of the Imams to the contrary, as you have learned. And at the lower level, the indication of the evidence with the branching of branches and the probability of expressions, rather their appearance, is sufficient for the correctness of the attribution, so understand. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Ghazali (may Allah have mercy on him) took to suspension (waqf) due to lack of knowledge of whether it is a reality in either the last only or all. Al-Murtada from the Rawafid said it is shared (ishtirak) between them, so it depends on the appearance of a qarina. The commentator of al-Mukhtasar said: These two opinions—suspension and sharedness—agree with us in the ruling because they require attribution to the last and suspension regarding others until evidence is found, even though they differ from us in the basis (makhadh). For their basis in specifying the last is certainty (yaqeen), because if it is specifically for it, it is apparent; if it is for all, it also includes it. There is no possibility of it being only for others without a qarina. Similarly for sharedness. As for us, the basis is appearance (zuhur) in the last. Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili said: If there appears to be a break (idrab) from the first sentence, by differing in type—e.g., in being imperative, declarative, commanding, or prohibitive—or in name, such that the name suitable for exception differs, or in ruling, such that their ruling differs, e.g., 'Honor Banu Tamim and hire Mudar', while there is no pronoun in the second referring to the first, i.e., there is no pronoun in the second speech returning to the name mentioned in the first.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute regarding the operation of an exception (istithna) when it follows multiple sentences joined by coordinating conjunctions (waw, fa, thumma). The Hanafi position (aligned with grammarian Abu Ali al-Farisi) is that the exception applies only to the last clause. The Shafi'i position (aligned with Ibn Malik) is that it applies to all clauses. The dispute is about what is apparent (zahir), not about what is possible (imkan), because all possibilities (return to last, to all, to all except last, etc.) are linguistically possible. No party can claim the text is clear (nass) for one possibility. The author then notes that the Hanafi appearance in the last is recorded in Sharh al-Badi', and clarifies that Imam al-Nasafi and Ibn al-Humam did not explicitly assert appearance but only the ruling of returning to the last, leaving open the possibility of suspension. However, the evidence and usage support the attribution to Hanafis. Other scholars like al-Baqillani, al-Ghazali (suspension), and al-Murtada (shared) are noted; their rulings agree with Hanafis in effect (the last is included) but differ in basis. Finally, Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili adds conditions for the exception applying only to the last, such as when the clauses differ in type, name, or ruling, with no pronoun in the second referring to the first.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna): A linguistic and legal device that excludes an item from a general ruling using words like 'except' (illa).
ظهور (zuhur): Apparent meaning; the immediate understanding of a statement that is not decisively clear (nass) but is the most likely interpretation.
الجمل المتعاطفة (al-jumal al-mu'tatifah): Sentences joined by coordinating conjunctions like waw (and), fa (then), and thumma (then).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, if someone says 'Honor Banu Tamim and hire Mudar except Zaid', what is the effect of the exception?
A) Zaid is excluded from both honoring and hiring.
B) Zaid is excluded only from hiring Mudar, but must still be honored.
C) Zaid is excluded only from honoring Banu Tamim.
D) The exception is invalid because the clauses are not similar.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: an exception after coordinate clauses excludes only from the last clause, based on apparent meaning (zuhur), not certainty.

Hanafi Rule on Istithna' After Multiple Coordinated Sentences
Analyzing Hanafi, Shafi'i, and other views on exception after coordinated sentences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an exception after coordinated sentences attaches only to the last sentence, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position by analyzing the conditions of connection (ittisal) and apparent meaning (zuhur).

Core Idea
After multiple coordinated sentences, an exception applies only to the last unless a qarina forces wider application.

This lesson tackles a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the scope of an exception (istithna') when it appears after a series of coordinated sentences. The Hanafi school holds that the exception attaches only to the nearest sentence, while the Shafi'i school argues it applies to all unless there is a clear turning away (idrab). Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts where exceptions follow multiple commands or descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the Hanafi position
State the core question: Does an exception after coordinated sentences attach to all or only the last? Then state the Hanafi position: attaches only to the last.
This is based on the apparent meaning (zuhur) and the condition of connection (ittisal).
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil (appearance and connection)
The Hanafi argument: 1) The ruling of the first sentence is apparent and removal by exception is doubtful; the apparent is not opposed by doubt. 2) Connection is a condition of exception, and the exception is only connected to the last sentence.
The first proof relies on the principle that doubt does not overturn certainty or apparentness.
3
Understand the Shafi'i counter-dalil
The Shafi'i position, supported by Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili, argues that exception can attach to all if there is no idrab and the purpose is shared, as in the Quranic verse of qadhf (24:4-5).
In the verse, the exception 'except those who repent' is argued to apply to all preceding sentences because the overall purpose is humiliation and retaliation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: The doubt about attachment to the first prevents ruling its removal; connection with the last is stronger because it is the nearest. The connection by mere conjunction is weak.
Thus the exception does not affect the first sentence without a clear qarina.
5
Apply the rule to a new case
Consider the statement: 'Honor the Banu Tamim and hire Mudar except Zayd.' Hanafi: 'except Zayd' only excludes Zayd from hiring, not from honor. Shafi'i: excludes from both unless a qarina indicates otherwise.
This shows the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two schools.

فصل بخلاف الشافعية فإنهم لم يقصروا فالخلاف إن كان ففي تعيين الموانع وهو أمر آخر لنا أولاً أن حكم الأولى ظاهر في الثبوت عموماً (ورفعه عن البعض بالاستثناء مشكوك لجواز كونه للأخيرة فقط) فلا يرفع حكم بها، وحينئذ فالتعلق بها إما مجاز أو حقيقة، وعلى الثاني الاشتراك، لأن المنفرد لا يحتاج إلى قرينة فتعين الأول، فلزم الظهور في التعلق بالأخيرة فتأمل فيه، ثم اعترض عليه بأن ظهور حكم الأولى ممنوع، بل وارتفاعه بالاستثناء مشكوك، لأن الكلام فيما لا صارف عن الأخيرة فيتعلق به، وهو القريب ولا يتعلق بما عداه إلا بقرينة، وهذا يدلّ على عدم التعلق بما عداها فتأمل فيه، فإنه موضع تأمل (و) لنا (ثانياً) الاتصال فقط ضعيف) لا يكفي لتعلق الاستثناء التحققه الصارف عنه فيعتبر بدليل آخر موجب لاعتبار هذا الاتصال، والسر في مع ضعف هذا الاتصال أن العطف في الجمل لا يفيد إلا تحققها في الواقع، وهذا حاصل إن لم
Issue: The exception after sentences coordinated by waw and the like of fa and thumma, as in al-Tahrir, attaches to the last only according to us, like Abu Ali al-Farisi among the grammarians, i.e., as he went to it. And it attaches to all, i.e., each one, according to the Shafi'is, like Ibn Malik among them. He said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: the dispute is in apparent meaning (zuhur). According to us, it is apparent in attaching to the last, and according to them, in attaching to all, not in possibility, because its return to all, i.e., each one, is established, and its return to other than the last and to the last only and to other than the first only is established. So no one can claim textual definitiveness in any one of the probabilities; only apparent meaning is suitable for dispute. Know that the apparent meaning in the last is stated in Sharh al-Badi'. It appears from the words of Imam al-Nasafi, may Allah have mercy on him, and what Sheikh Ibn al-Humam said that the Hanafis did not explicitly state it, but only stated the return to the last. It is possible that they were undecided about the apparent meaning, and what is attributed to the Shafi'is is taking their evidence. So testimony of negation must be corrected by thorough induction, and it is not the explicit statement of the imams to the contrary as you have learned. Even if we concede, the indication of the evidence with the branching of sub-issues and the probability of expressions, rather their apparentness, is sufficient for the correctness of attribution. So understand. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and the great Imam, the proof of Islam al-Ghazali, said: stopping (waqf) due to lack of knowledge whether it is literal in which one: the last only or in all. Al-Murtada from the Rafidites said: shared (ishtirak) in both, so one stops until appearance of a qarina. The commentator of al-Mukhtasar said: these two opinions, waqf and ishtirak, agree with us in the ruling because they necessitate attachment to the last and stopping in other than it until evidence is established, although they differ in derivation because their derivation for specifying the last is certainty, because if it is for it specifically, it is apparent, and if it is for all, it is also for it, and there is no probability of it being for other than it without a qarina. Similarly in ishtirak. As for us, the derivation is the apparent meaning in the last. Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili said: if turning away (idrab) from the first sentence is apparent, either by differing in type: declarative and performative and imperative and prohibitive, or in noun: that the noun suitable for the exception from is different, or in ruling: that their rulings are different, like 'Honor the Banu Tamim and hire Mudar', while there is no pronoun of the first in the second, i.e., in the second speech there is no pronoun referring to the noun mentioned in the first suitable for exception from, like 'Honor the Banu Tamim and hire them except Zayd', and there is no sharing between them in the purpose for which they are stated, then it is for the last, i.e., when turning away is apparent, it is for the last. Otherwise, turning away is apparent either by not differing in type, noun, and ruling, or by differing in one of them but there is a pronoun of the first in the second, or by differing without a pronoun of the first in the second but they share in the purpose, then it is for all, i.e., it is for all in the three forms. Among them is the verse of qadhf: 'And those who accuse chaste women then do not bring four witnesses, flog them eighty stripes and do not accept their testimony ever, and those are the transgressors, except those who repent' [24:4-5] because the purpose of the sentences, which is humiliation and retaliation, is one. So he, Abu al-Husayn, agrees with the Shafi'is, because the result of his words is attachment to all except for an impediment, which is the Shafi'is' statement, except that he limited the impediment to what he detailed, unlike the Shafi'is who did not limit. So the disagreement, if any, is in specifying impediments, which is another matter. First for us: the ruling of the first is apparently established generally, and its removal from some by exception is doubtful because it may be for the last only, so it does not remove the ruling of the first, just as it may attach to all and thus remove the ruling of the first. When removal is doubtful, it does not oppose it because the apparent is not opposed by the doubtful. This is better than what they said: the ruling of the first is certain and its removal by exception is doubtful; for it is open to objection that certainty of the ruling of the first is denied, because probability of its removal by exception even with an existent turn-off exists, and there is no certainty with probability, although it may be answered that what is meant by certainty is appearance. So reflect. Unlike the last, for its ruling is not apparent because removal is apparent in it, since speech is concerning where there is no turn-off from it; then it attaches to it. Hence it is necessary by agreement. And when it is established that the last is apparent in removal, then what is in al-Mukhtasar that the last is also like that, i.e., its ruling is apparent and removal by exception is doubtful due to possibility of its return to the first by evidence, so it does not remove the last, is refuted. The refutation is apparent, and this evidence apparently does not indicate non-attachment to other than the last, but rather stopping. And it may be formulated that removal of the first is doubtful, so it is not removed except when a qarina for its attachment appears; then its attachment is either metaphorical or literal; on the second is sharedness, because the unique does not need a qarina; so the first is determined, so apparentness in attachment to the last is necessary. So reflect on it. Then it was objected to that appearance of the ruling of the first is denied, rather its removal by exception is apparent to the opponent; how is this and it is in the strength of the very desired? And you may formulate thus: attachment of dependents to the near is an established principle among Arabic scholars, and it may be deviated from as well. So the ruling of the first is apparently established due to non-attachment of the modifier to it, and its removal by exception is doubtful because speech concerns where there is no turn-off from the last, so it attaches to it, which is the near, and does not attach to other than it except with a qarina. This indicates non-attachment to other than it. So reflect on it, for it is a place of reflection. And second for us: connection (ittisal) is a condition of exception, and it is in the last only, because it is later than the first by being taken in another sentence, so it does not attach to other than it. This indicates non-attachment to other than the last. If you say: connection by conjunction exists, he said: connection by conjunction only is weak, insufficient for attachment of the exception due to the turn-off from it; so another evidence necessitating consideration of this connection is considered. The secret behind the weakness of this connection is that conjunction in sentences only serves to establish them in reality, and this is obtained if not continued.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the rule of istithna' (exception) when it appears after multiple coordinated sentences. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa's school, is that the exception attaches only to the last sentence. This is based on apparent meaning (zuhur) and the condition of connection (ittisal). The Shafi'i position, attributed to Ibn Malik, is that the exception attaches to all sentences unless there is a qarina indicating otherwise. The passage also presents the views of other scholars like al-Baqillani and al-Ghazali, who stopped (waqf), and al-Murtada who said it is shared (ishtirak). Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili proposed a detailed criterion based on the presence of 'idrab' (turning away) and other factors, using the Quranic verse of qadhf (24:4-5) as an example where the exception applies to all sentences because the purpose is one. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the ruling of the first sentence is apparent and its removal by exception is doubtful, so doubt cannot override apparentness. Additionally, the condition of connection requires the exception to be immediately connected to what is excepted from, and since the last sentence is the nearest, it takes precedence. The passage concludes with a discussion of the weakness of connection by mere conjunction. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as transmitted in Sharh al-Tahrir and works of later Hanafis like Ibn al-Humam.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): An exception clause using 'illa' (except) that excludes some individuals from the ruling of the preceding statement.
جمل متعاطفة (jumal muta'atifa): Multiple sentences coordinated by particles like waw, fa, or thumma.
ظهور (zuhur): Apparent meaning that is not definitive but is strong enough to be the basis for legal ruling in the absence of a counter-indication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, to which sentence does an exception (istithna') attach when it appears after multiple sentences coordinated by waw?
A) To all sentences equally.
B) Only to the last sentence.
C) Only to the first sentence.
D) It is undetermined and must be decided by context.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an exception after coordinated sentences defaults to attachment to the last sentence, based on connection and the protection of apparent rulings from doubtful removal.

The Hanafi Position: Istithna' Attaches Only to the Last Clause
Dispute over exception after conjoined sentences: Hanafi arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on istithna' after conjoined sentences and apply the proof from the 'ten except four except two' example.

Core Idea
An exception after conjoined clauses applies only to the last clause by default unless a contextual indicator (qarina) extends it.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi school's ruling on how an exception (istithna') operates when it follows a series of sentences linked by conjunctions like 'wa' (and). The central question: does the exception cut across all the clauses, or only the immediately preceding one? The Hanafis argue that, without a contextual clue, the exception restricts only the final clause, based on linguistic custom and logical necessity. We will examine the key proofs, including the famous 'ten except four except two' example, and understand why the Hanafis consider this position the strongest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the Hanafi position
Recognize that the dispute is about the scope of an exception (istithna') after a series of conjoined sentences (ma'tufat). The Hanafi position is that it applies to the last clause only, unless there is a qarina (contextual indicator) to extend it to all.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from custom and silence
Examine the argument that customary connection (al-ittisal al-'urfi) is insufficient; the real condition for exception to retroact to earlier clauses is that the speaker did not pause or start a new speech. In conjoined sentences, the speaker does start a new speech after the conjunction, so exception only attaches to the last.
Key terms: al-ittisal al-'urfi, sukun (silence), akhdh fi kalam akhar (starting another speech).
3
Analyze the proof from the 'ten except four except two' example
Understand the qiyas: if someone says 'I owe ten except four except two', the result is eight (ten minus two), not six (ten minus four minus two). This shows the second exception attaches only to the immediately preceding clause ('except four'), not to the entire statement. Thus, exception does not revert to all previous clauses.
This is a straightforward demonstration that exception follows the nearest antecedent.
4
Examine the Hanafi response to potential objections
Review Hanafi rebuttals to objections, such as the claim that customary connection is sufficient. The Hanafis argue that this would lead to inconsistency, as lengthy conjoined sentences are not considered one speech unit. Also, the example of 'in sha Allah' is distinguished as a condition (shart), not an exception.
The Hanafis maintain that necessity (darura) only requires attachment to the last clause.
5
Summarize the relied-upon tarjih
Conclude that the Hanafi position is the preponderant (al-mukhtar) one, supported by multiple proofs: custom, the 'ten except four except two' example, avoidance of tanazu' (conflict of two actions on one object), and the analysis of necessity.
