المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
أفراد الشرط، فإنها أيضاً علة لفاعلية الفاعل، وكونه تاماً في الجاهلية فتدبر، فإن فيه تأملاً فإن الغائية ليست مما يتوقف عليه وجود المعلول إلا بالعرض لبطلان العبث والشرط مما يتوقف عليه وجود المعلول نفسه، والفاعل ليس فاعلاً تاماً دونه فتدبر (و) أورد (ثانياً أنه منقوض بجزء السبب فإنه لا يوجد المسبب دونه ولا يلزم أن يوجد عنده، واعلم أنه لا يتوجه إلى التعريف فإنه لا يصدق عليه لا يوجد المشروط دونه وإن صدق لا يوجد المسبب دونه ، وإذ قد أريد بالمشروط الشيء لدفع الدور توجه إليه هذا الإيراد فهذا في الحقيقة إيراد على جواب الدور (ويجاب : بأن جزء السبب قد يوجد المسبب دونه إذا وجد بسبب آخر غير الذي هذا الجزء جزؤه فلا يصدق عليه الحد قيل : هذا الجواب في غاية السقوط، لأن المراد) في النقض (جزء السبب المتحد أي الواحد للمسبب على ما صرح به الآمدي) ويصدق عليه أنه لا يوجد المسبب دونه (وأجيب) المراد عدم الوجود بدونه لنوعه أي لنوع الارتباط الذي بينه وبين الشيء والحاصل الشرط الأمر المتعلق بالشيء لا يوجد بدونه لنوع هذا التعلق، ولا يلزم يوجد عنده حتى يتناول الشرط الشبيه بالسبب) وهو الشرط الذي يستتبع المشروط وهذا إذا كان آخر ما يتوقف عليه فإنه يلزمه وجود المشروط، لكن لا لنوع تعلقه بالمشروط، وإلا لكانت سائر الشروط مستلزمة، فلو لم يرد لنوعه لخرج هذا الشرط، ثم من الأسباب ما له شرطية، كما يقال : الوقت شرط لصحة الجمعة والعيدين والأداء مطلقاً، وبهذه العناية يندفع النقض به فإن للوقت علاقتين علاقة الاقتضاء وبهذه العلاقة سبب وعلاقة الشرطية ويصدق عليه أنه متعلق بالجملة التي لا توجد بدونه بنوع هذه العلاقة ويمكن أن يتخلف وجودها عنه فاندفع النقض بهذه الشروط التي هي الأسباب، ثم هذا بحسب الجليل من النظر، والنظر الدقيق فيها أن ما هو سبب لشيء لا يكون شرطاً أصلاً إذ الشرط لا اقتضاء فيه أصلاً، ولا يتوقف وجوده على وجود السبب إلا من جهة خصوص المادة وأما الوقت فليس سبباً لوجود صلاة الجمعة وأدائها وإنما هو سبب لوجوبها وافتراضها والشرطية إنما هي بالنسبة إلى الأداء والوجود، ولا استحالة في كون شيء سبباً لشيء وشرطاً لآخر فافهم. إذا تقرر أن المراد هذا شرع في تقرير الجواب وقال (وعدم وجود المسبب بدون جزء السبب المتحد إنما هو بالنظر إلى خصوص وهو كونه متحداً (لا) بالنظر إلى تعلق السببية (مطلقاً) وإلا يلزم أن لا يوجد بدون شيء من الأسباب ولو متعدداً لكن يلزم حينئذ أن لا يكون للقيد الثاني) وهو قوله : ولا يلزم أن يوجد عنده (فائدة فإن السبب يخرج حينئذ بالقيد (الأول) وكان هذا الإخراج السبب، فإن قلت : لا انحصار لفائدته في إخراجه، بل يجوز أن يكون الفائدة إخراج العلة فإنه لا يوجد المعلول دونه ، قلت : سيجيء أن العلل تتعدد كالسبب فليس عدم الوجود دونها لنوع العلية، فخرجت بالأول، فتدبر فيه إلا أن يقال ذلك لإخراج القدر المشترك بين مجموع الأسباب) فإنه المسبب دون القدر المشترك لنوعه، لكن فيه مناقشة فإنه إنما لا يوجد دون القدر المشترك الأسباب لا لنوع تعلقه بالمسبب، فإنه ليس غير السببية (أقول : بقي أن الشرط قد يكون شرطاً) لشيء مع سبب) له (دون) سبب آخر ، كما أن القبض شرط للملك في الهبة دون البيع فإنه يفيد الملك بنفس العقد دون الهبة (فلو قطع النظر عن خصوص السبب) ويؤخذ شرط للملك مثلاً (خرج ذلك الحد) لأنه يوجد للمشروط دونه بل لا يصدق الحد على شرط أصلاً، فإن نوع الشرطية لم يأب عن وجود المشروط دونه (إلا أن يقال) ليس المحدود الشرط العام بل المحدود شرط الشيء (مطلقاً أي من كل وجه مع كل سبب (وهذا) المذكور (شرط من وجه دون وجه فتدبر) والحق في الجواب أن كون القبض شرط الملك ممنوع، وإنما هو شرط لحصة الملك الحاصلة من الهبة ولا يلزم من اشتراط الخاص بشيء اشتراط المطلق به، بل هو شرط إيجاب الهبة الملك وقبله لم يوجد السبب تاماً، كما يفصح عنه عبارات الفقهاء فتدبر فإن قلت ما وجه قولهم : الشرط لا يتعدد) بدلاً بأن يكون لمشروط واحد شروط متعددة يوجد هذا المشروط تارة مع هذا الشرط وتارة مع آخر (والسبب يتعدد) على هذا النمط، فإن الملك يحدث بأسباب شتى، ولم يرد أن الشرط لا يتعدد أصلاً حتى يرد عليه أن تعدد الشرط بديهي، ولم يقل أحد أن الشرط لا يتعدد قلت : المعتبر في مفهوم الشرط اصطلاحاً عدم الوجود بدونه فلا يمكن التعدد المذكور وإلا وجد المشروط بدون كل (فعند التعدد) بحسب الظاهر (الشرط القدر المشترك بين الشروط المتعددة (و) المعتبر (في مفهوم السبب استتباع الوجود وكل واحد معين من الأسباب المتعددة بدلاً (كذلك) أي مستتبع لوجود المعلول، كالجناية على الصوم والظهار مفضيان إلى وجوب الكفارة (والسر فيه) أي في اعتبار القدر المشترك في الشروط دون الأسباب ما تقرر) في العلوم العقلية أن فاعل الواحد بالعدد لا بد أن يكون واحداً بالعدد إذ لولاه لجاز فاعلية الواحد بالشخص للواحد بالعموم (إذ العقل ينقبض عن أن يكون تحصيل الفاعل دون تحصيل (معلوله والأسباب بمنزلة الفواعل فلا يجوز أن تكون قدراً مشتركاً، وإلا لكان الواحد بالعموم الأضعف سبباً وفاعلاً للواحد بالعدد الأقوى بخلاف الشرط) فإنه لا انقباض عن كون الأقوى تحصيلاً متوقفاً على الأضعف فيه لعدم كونه متحصلاً بخلاف الفاعل هذا، ويرد عليه أن امتناع كون تحصيل الفاعل أضعف إنما في الفاعل الحقيقي المؤثر دون المؤثر الجعلي فلا يتم هذا السر والأولى الاكتفاء بما سبق (أقول خلاصة ذلك) أن الواحد بالعموم لا يكون فاعلاً للمتشخص (منقوض باقتضاء الماهية فرداً معيناً كالواجب عند المتكلمين الذاهبين إلى زيادة التشخص فإنه متعين بنفسه ومعلول للماهية الواجبة (والعقل) على رأي الفلاسفة الذاهبين إلى انحصار نوعه في شخصه لاقتضائه التشخص بنفسه (فتأمل) بل نقول اقتضاء الماهية التشخص غير معقول، لأن نسبتها إلى الأشخاص على السواء، والمقتضى لا يكون متساوي النسبة إلى المعلول وغيره وأيضاً جاعل الوجود والتشخص واحد بل التشخص نحو الوجود على التحقيق، فلو اقتضى الماهية التشخص لاقتضت الوجود، فيوجد قبل الوجود، وبهذا أبطل الفلاسفة زيادة الوجود والتعين عليه سبحانه ونسبة علية الماهية للعقل التعين لا يقبلها النقاد من المهرة والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال
The third article on linguistic principles: ... The condition is that the thing is not found without it by the type of attachment, and it is not necessary that it be found with it. ... It is said in the Minhaj: 'The condition is that upon which the effect's influence depends, whether rational or conventional.' ...
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut is a detailed analysis of the definition of 'shart' (condition) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It opens with the third linguistic article. The author first presents the definition: a condition is something related to a thing in such a way that the thing does not exist without it due to the type of that relation, but its existence does not necessitate the thing's existence. This distinguishes a condition from a cause (sabab), which inherently entails its effect. The passage then addresses objections: first, the objection that part of the cause (juz' al-sabab) also fits this definition since the effect does not exist without it but may exist with it (if another cause brings the effect). The response clarifies that the definition considers the type of relation, not the specific part. Second, the objection regarding time as a condition for Friday prayer and Eid: time has two relations—a causal relation for obligation and a conditional relation for performance. Thus it does not undermine the definition. The text then explores deeper: a condition has no inherent causality; it merely makes the cause effective. The discussion includes the example of qabd (possession) in gift (hiba) versus sale (bay'). In Hanafi law, qabd is a condition for ownership in gift but not in sale. The response argues that qabd is actually a condition for the specific ownership arising from gift, not for ownership absolutely. Finally, the passage addresses why conditions cannot be multiple for a single conditioned thing: the definition of condition entails non-existence without it, so if multiple were possible, the conditioned could exist without each, contradicting the definition. Instead, when multiple apparent conditions exist, the actual condition is the common factor among them. Causes, however, can multiply because each individually entails the effect. The philosophical reason is that a single effect requires a single efficient cause; causes are like agents, and a single agent cannot be a common factor. Conditions are mere prerequisites and do not share this property. The text concludes with a critical reflection on this philosophical argument, noting it applies only to real efficient causes, not conventional causes like legal causes.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): An entity related to another such that the latter does not exist without it due to the type of that relation, but its existence does not necessitate the latter.
السبب (al-sabab): That which entails the existence of its effect; its presence necessitates the effect unless a preventer exists.
جزء السبب (juz' al-sabab): A part of the cause; the effect does not exist without that part if it is part of a single cause, but may exist via another cause.
القبض (al-qabd): Possession or taking delivery; in the example, a condition for ownership in gift (hiba) but not in sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best defines a 'shart' (condition) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh?
A) That without which the conditioned does not exist, and whose existence necessitates the conditioned.
B) That without which the conditioned does not exist due to the type of relation, but whose existence does not necessitate the conditioned.
C) That which entails the existence of its effect and is always a cause.
D) That which is a part of the cause and always prevents the effect if absent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of shart is precise: a condition is necessary for the existence of the conditioned due to a specific relation, but it does not cause the conditioned; that is reserved for sabab.

The Threefold Classification of Conditions in Hanafi Usul
Classification of conditions and results into singular, collective, and alternative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify conditions as singular, collective, or alternative, and distinguish between rational and legal conditions.

Core Idea
Conditions are three types: singular, collective, alternative — and the result mirrors this.

Conditions (shurut) are central to legal reasoning. In this passage, the author clarifies the types of conditions: rational vs. legal, and then the key threefold division: singular, collective (all together), and alternative (any one). Understanding this taxonomy will help you analyze conditional statements in usul and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish rational vs. legal conditions
Identify as rational if reason dictates it (e.g., substance for accident); as legal if revelation establishes it (e.g., purity for prayer).
This distinction is foundational but not the main focus.
2
Understand the three types of conditions
A condition may be singular (one specific thing), collective (a set all required together), or alternative (any one of several suffices).
Memorize: wahid, jam', badal.
3
Apply the same division to results (jaza')
Just as conditions can be three types, so can the result: singular, collective, alternative.
This yields 9 combinations (3x3) used in analyzing conditional statements.
4
Reflect on the logical implication
Note that a condition is a sign (not a cause) — its presence does not guarantee the result, but its absence prevents it.
This nuance is key in distinguishing shart from sabab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هو أي الشرط المذكور عقلي يحكم بشرطيته العقل كالجوهر للعرض فإن العرض لا يوجد بدون الجوهر وشرعي يحكم بشرطيته الشرع كالطهارة للصلاة فإنها لا توجد دونها وأما الشرط لغة فهو العلامة، ومنه أشراط الساعة أي علاماتها فيه إشارة إلى أن الشرط اللغوي لا يصلح قسماً منه كما زعم ابن الحاجب وإلى أن الشرط اللغوي العلامة لا مدخول أن وأخواتها كما زعمه أيضاً وأما تسمية النحاة مدخول أن وأخواتها شرطاً فلصيرورته علامة على الجزاء هذا وجه التسمية إذ كثيراً ما يستعمل إن فيما لا يتوقف المسبب بعده على غيره فهو علة موجبة فيستلزم وجوده لوجوده أي وجود مدخول أن وجود المسبب فهو علامة على الجزاء لا نفيه لنفيه أي لا يستلزم نفي مدخول أن نفي المسبب لاحتمال أن يوجد من سبب آخر بأن يكون المسبب أعم منه ، ويكون لازماً له ولسبب آخر ولا يلزم توارد السببين على أثر واحد بالشخص لأنه لعمومه لا يكون واحداً شخصياً، نعم : إذا كان مساوياً له يلزم من نفيه نفيه ولهذا ينتج في الاستثنائي أي المتصل وضع المقدم منه لوضع التالي منه لا نفيه لنفيه ينتج نفي المقدم لنفي التالي وهو أي الشرط قد يتحد وقد يتعدد جمعاً بأن يكون الشرط المجموع من حيث المجموع أو يتعدد بدلاً بأن يكون الشرط واحداً لا بعينه من أمور متعددة فهذه ثلاثة من الأقسام وكذا الجزاء قد يتحد وقد يتعدد جمعاً وقد يتعدد بدلاً فالمجموع تسعة حاصلة من ضرب الثلاثة في الثلاثة.
Then, it (i.e., the mentioned condition) is rational: reason judges its conditionality, like substance for accident, for the accident does not exist without substance; and legal: law judges its conditionality, like purity for prayer, for it does not exist without it. As for condition linguistically, it is a sign, e.g., 'signs of the Hour' meaning its marks. Therein is an indication that the linguistic condition is not valid as a division thereof, as Ibn al-Hajib claimed, and that the linguistic condition is the sign, not the governed of 'in' and its sisters, as he also claimed. As for grammarians calling the governed of 'in' and its sisters a 'condition', it is because it becomes a sign for the result. That is the reason for the naming, since 'in' is often used where the consequent does not depend on something else; it is an efficient cause that entails its existence by its existence, i.e., the existence of the governed of 'in' entails the existence of the consequent, so it is a sign for the result, not the negation by its negation, i.e., the negation of the governed of 'in' does not entail the negation of the consequent because it may exist from another cause, such that the consequent is more general than it, and it is a necessary concomitant of it and of another cause. And it does not necessitate two causes converging on one effect in person because due to its generality it is not a single personal effect. Yes, if it is equal to it, its negation entails its negation. Hence, in the conditional (i.e., connected) syllogism, positing the antecedent entails positing the consequent, not negation by negation; it entails negation of antecedent by negation of consequent. And it (i.e., the condition) may be singular, or multiple collectively, i.e., the condition is the sum as a whole, or multiple alternatively, i.e., the condition is a single indeterminate one among multiple things. These are three divisions. So too the result may be singular, or multiple collectively, or multiple alternatively. The total is nine, resulting from multiplying three by three.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of conditions and results in Hanafi jurisprudence. It first distinguishes rational conditions (like substance for accident) from legal conditions (like purity for prayer). It then explains the linguistic meaning of 'condition' as a sign, rejecting Ibn al-Hajib's claim. The core taxonomy: a condition can be singular (one specific thing), multiple collectively (all required together), or multiple alternatively (any one of several). The same applies to the result. Multiplying the three types of conditions by three types of results yields nine possible combinations, used to analyze conditional statements.

Key Terms
الشرط العقلي (al-shart al-'aqli): A condition that reason dictates, such as substance for accident.
الشرط الشرعي (al-shart al-shar'i): A condition established by divine law, like purity for prayer.
بدلاً (badalan): Alternative condition: any one of multiple options suffices.
جمعاً (jam'an): Collective condition: all must be present together.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many types of conditions are recognized in this text?
A) Two: rational and legal
B) Three: singular, collective, and alternative
C) Four: linguistic, rational, legal, and grammatical
D) Nine: combining types of condition and result

Key Takeaway
A condition in usul can be singular, collective, or alternative, and the result follows the same pattern, producing nine logical combinations.

Ghayah and Shart: Connected Specifiers in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the rule of ghayah (limit) and shart (condition) as connected specifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ghayah/shart and true takhsis, and state the Hanafi position on their return when following multiple conjoined sentences.

Core Idea
Ghayah and shart are not true specifiers; they affect determinations (taqdir), not individuals.

In this passage, the author expands on the third type of connected specifiers: the limit (ghayah) and the condition (shart). While superficially similar to exception, they operate differently in Hanafi usul. We will explore the dispute over whether they return to all or only the last of multiple conjoined sentences, and then delve into the deeper issue of whether they truly constitute takhsis (specification of individuals).

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic nature of ghayah and shart
Understand that ghayah is expressed by 'ila' or 'hatta' and shart by 'in' or similar particles. They both restrict the application of a ruling to a specific condition or limit, not by excluding individuals but by specifying the circumstances under which the ruling applies.
Example: 'Honor the Banu Tamim until they enter' – the limit is the entry.
2
Understand the dispute on return to multiple sentences
When a ghayah or shart follows multiple conjoined sentences, does it affect all sentences or only the last? The Hanafi position (mukhtar) is that it returns to the last only, while the Shafi'is analogize it to exception, returning to all.
The passage cites various scholars: Hujjat al-Islam and al-Qadi suspend judgment; al-Rafidi says it is common; Abu al-Husayn distinguishes based on idrab (shift).
3
Analyze the definition of takhsis and why ghayah/shart do not qualify
Takhsis is defined as extracting some individuals of the general term from the ruling. Ghayah and shart do not extract individuals; they indicate that the ruling does not apply under certain determinations (taqdir) – e.g., when the condition is absent or after the limit. Thus, they are not true specifiers in the systematic (waḍʿī) sense.
The author notes that if one rephrases them as 'the ruling applies on some determinations' (existence of condition, before limit), it would still not be extracting individuals.
4
Recognize the occasional specification scenario
Sometimes a condition or limit can incidentally exclude individuals, as in 'Honor the Arabs if they are Hashimites' – this extracts non-Hashimites. But this is occasional (ittifaqi), not the primary (waḍʿī) function.
The author points out that claiming occasional specification would open the door to many other particles (like 'bal', 'la', adverbs) being considered specifiers.
5
Grasp the author's response to the Shafi'i claim
The Shafi'is argue that shart and ghayah are specifiers by analogy to exception. The author responds by showing that their primary function is not to extract individuals, and the occasional extraction is not the systematic purpose. The discussion in usul is about the primary (waḍʿī) function, not accidental outcomes.
This clarifies the Hanafi position and why these are classified differently from exception.

الثالث • المخصصات المتصلة الغاية ولفظها إلى وحتى وقد مرا في حروف المعاني من (نحو): أكرم بني تميم إلى أن يدخلوا وهي كالشرطة اتحاداً وتعدداً) فقد تكون واحداً ومتعدداً اجتماعاً أو بدلاً (و) هي (كالاستثناء في العود إلى الجميع أو إلى الأخيرة) إذا عقبت بعد جمل متعاطفة والمذاهب) ههنا هي (المذاهب) المذكورة ثمة (والمختار) ههنا (المختار) ثمة فالمختار عندنا الانصراف إلى الأخيرة وعند الشافعية إلى الكل، وحجة الإسلام قدس سره والقاضي يتوقفان والرافضي مشترك فيهما وأبو الحسين إن ظهر الإضراب فللأخيرة وإلا فللكل في «التحرير» لا يخفى عدم صدق تعريف التخصيص على إخراج الشرط والغاية لعدم إخراج شيء منهما بعض المسمى من أفراد العام فإن مفادهما عدم ثبوت الحكم على بعض التقادير) وهي تقدير فقدان الشرط وما بعد الغاية لا عدم ثبوت الحكم لبعض الإفراد حتى يكون تخصيصاً، ثم إنه لو قال مفادهما ثبوت الحكم على بعض التقادير وهو تقدير وجود الشرط وقبل الغاية لكان متأتياً على مذهبنا أيضاً، لكن لما كان دعوى الشافعية أنهما مخصصان تنزل إلى رأيهم وقال : مفادهما عدم ثبوت الحكم على بعض التقادير (أقول) في جوابه (قد يخرج) الشرط أو الغاية بعض المسمى عن الحكم (دائماً) لا على بعض التقادير، فصار العام بهما (ونحو: أكرم العرب إن كان (هاشمياً فأخرج الشرط غير الهاشمي (وأكرم وصاً المسلمين إلى القرن الثالث فأخرج مسلمي هذا الزمان وفيه ما فيه لأن هذا التخصيص اتفاقي والكلام كان في الوضعي المطرد، وإليه أشار في «التحرير» أيضاً، فإنه قال في أثناء هذا البحث وإن كان قد يتفق معه تخصيص آخر، وقد لا وقد يتضادان، أي قد يتفق قصر التقديرات تخصيص آخر هو قصر الإفراد وقد لا يتفق وقد يتضادان، فإن قلت : القوم العادون إياهما من المخصصات لم يريدوا التخصيص بهما دائماً بل في بعض الأحيان، قلت: ظاهر كلامهم دعوى وضعهما للتخصيص كالاستثناء، ولو كان مرادهم التخصيص ولو اتفاقاً لم ينحصر في هذه الخمسة، بل قد يوجد في غيرها من المتصلات الغير المستقلة نحو كلمة : بل، ولا العاطفة والظرف فتدبر .
Third: The connected specifiers – the ghayah (limit) and its words are 'ila' and 'hatta'. They have both been mentioned in the letters of meanings. (Example): 'Honor the Banu Tamim until they enter.' And they are like the condition in unity and multiplicity, so they may be one or multiple, together or alternatively. And they are like the exception in returning to all or to the last if they follow conjoined sentences. The positions here are the positions mentioned there. And the preferred here is the preferred there. So the preferred with us is the return to the last, and with the Shafi'is to all. And Hujjat al-Islam (may his secret be sanctified) and al-Qadi suspend judgment, and al-Rafiḍi says it is common between them, and Abu al-Husayn: if there is a shift (iḍrab) then for the last, otherwise for all. In 'al-Tahrir': It is not hidden that the definition of takhsis does not apply to the extraction of the shart and ghayah because they do not extract any part of the named from the individuals of the general. For their purport is the non-establishment of the ruling on some determinations, which are the estimation of the absence of the condition and what is after the ghayah, not the non-establishment for some individuals so that it would be takhsis. Then, if one were to say their purport is the establishment of the ruling on some determinations, namely the estimation of the existence of the condition and before the ghayah, it would be feasible according to our school as well. But since the Shafi'is claim that they are specifiers, he descended to their view and said: Their purport is the non-establishment of the ruling on some determinations. I say in response: It may be that the shart or ghayah extracts some of the named from the ruling always, not on some determinations, so the general becomes specified by them. And like: 'Honor the Arabs if they are Hashimites' – the condition extracts the non-Hashimite. And 'Honor the Muslims up to the third century' – it extracts the Muslims of this time. But there is something in it, because this specification is occasional, and the discussion was about the systematic (waḍʿī). And 'al-Tahrir' also pointed to this, for he said in the midst of this research that it may coincide with it another specification, and may not, and they may be contradictory. That is, it may coincide with a restriction of determinations another specification that is a restriction of individuals, and may not coincide, and may be contradictory. If you say: The people who count them among the specifiers did not intend specification by them always but sometimes, I say: The apparent meaning of their speech is the claim of their being posited for specification like the exception. And if their intention was specification even occasionally, it would not be confined to these five, but may be found in other non-independent connected (specifiers) like the word 'bal', and the 'la' of negation, and the adverb, so reflect.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the third type of connected specifiers (مخصصات متصلة): the ghayah (limit) expressed by 'ila' and 'hatta', and the shart (condition). The author first discusses their operation when following multiple conjoined sentences: like exception, they may return to all or only the last. The Hanafi position is that they return only to the last, while the Shafi'is say to all. Various scholars' opinions are noted. Then the author addresses a deeper issue: whether these truly constitute takhsis (specification). He argues that according to the definition of takhsis (extracting some individuals of the general), the shart and ghayah do not do that because their meaning is that the ruling does not apply under certain circumstances (condition absent or beyond the limit), not that some individuals are excluded. Thus, they are not really specifiers in the strict sense. However, occasionally they can function to exclude individuals, as in 'Honor the Arabs if they are Hashimites' where non-Hashimites are excluded, but this is incidental (ittifaqi) rather than the systematic (waḍʿi) purpose. The author concludes that those who count them among the specifiers generally claim they are posited for specification, otherwise many other particles would qualify.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): A limit or endpoint expressed by the particles 'ila' or 'hatta', indicating that the ruling applies until that point is reached.
شرط (sharṭ): A condition attached to the ruling, such that the ruling applies only when the condition is met.
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): The act of specifying a general term, extracting some of its individuals from the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the preferred ruling when a ghayah (limit) follows multiple conjoined sentences?
A) It returns to all sentences
B) It returns only to the last sentence
C) It suspends judgment
D) It is common between all

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, conditions and limits are not true specifiers (takhsis) of individuals but restrict the ruling to certain circumstances (taqdir).

Sifah as a Connected Specifier: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Explaining the adjective as a connected specifier and the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on its nature as takhsis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on sifah as a takhsis, distinguish it from the Shafi'i view, and evaluate the linguistic arguments used by each school.

Core Idea
The adjective restricts the genus, not the general; the general remains operative on all qualified individuals.

In this lesson, we delve into a core usul al-fiqh dispute: whether adjectives (sifah) and other restrictions like conditions and limits are true specifiers (mukhassisat) of a general command. The Hanafi school offers a nuanced position that these restrictions do not withdraw the ruling from any individuals but rather redefine the scope in a way that preserves the general's literal meaning. This dispute hinges on the acceptance or rejection of mafhum al-mukhalafah (contradictory implication). By the end, you will understand why the Hanafis reject these as takhsis in the strict sense and how that affects legal derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: Are connected specifiers (sifah, shart, ghayah) true takhsis of the general, withdrawing the ruling from some individuals?
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis hold that the general remains used in its full meaning; the adjective restricts the genus, not the individuals. The general's ruling applies to all individuals that satisfy the description.
The general word is not restricted in its application; rather, the description defines the subject matter.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position
Shafi'is rely on mafhum al-mukhalafah: the adjective implies that the ruling is absent for unsaid individuals, thus it acts as a specifier withdrawing the ruling from some.
4
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi rebuttal argues that treating the adjective as a specifier leads to tautology (e.g., 'knowledgeable men who are knowledgeable'). Moreover, restrictions are not independent; they only serve emphasis.
The author also argues that the Shafi'i view makes the restriction an independent specifier, which is linguistically implausible.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis apply the general command to all individuals that meet the description, while Shafi'is understand that the command does not extend to individuals not mentioned.
For example, 'Honor the knowledgeable men': Hanafi says honor all knowledgeable men (the general 'men' is not restricted), Shafi'i says only knowledgeable men are honored, ignorant are excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الوصف (في تعقبه المتعددة المعطوفة بعضها على بعض كتميم) وقريش الطوال كالاستثناء في تعقبه الجمل المتعاطفة مذهباً ومختاراً . واعلم أن التخصيص بالشرط والغاية والصفة إنما هو عند القائلين بالمفهوم المخالف) ما يوجد فيه الشرط والصفة كان المعنى أكرم الرجال العلماء إن كانوا علماء، أو أكرم الرجال العلماء العلماء، وهو كما ترى بل لا يبقى للشرط وغيره من القيود معنى سوى التأكيد بخلافهم، فإن معناها عندهم الحكم المخالف في المسكوت .هذا ثم إن مذهب الشافعية لا يكاد يصح بوجه أما أوّلاً فلأنه لو كان المراد بالعام الإفراد التي يوجد فيها الشرط أو الصفة أو المغيا بالغاية لفهم التكرار والوجدان أن يكذبه، وأما ثانياً فلأن هذه القيود غير مستقلة لا تفيد المعنى إلا بعد تعلقه بما تقدم ولا يصلح للتعلق إلا بطريق التأكيد، فيكون للقيود فائدة سوى نفي الحكم فلا يثبت المفهوم لفقد ما شرطوا لثبوته فافهم واستقم ثم إنك قد دريت أن في الاستثناء أيضاً العام باقٍ على معناه، وإذا قيد بالإخراج فهم من المركب معنى يصدق على الباقي بالوضع النوعي الذي للمركبات فهو أيضاً ليس تخصيصاً، وإنما طواه صاحب التحرير قدس سره لأنه اختار فيه ما اختاره المصنف من أن المراد بالصدر الباقي والاستثناء قرينة، فقد ظهر أن ما عده الشافعية من المتصلات مخصصاً ليس فيه قصر أصلاً والحق ما ذهب إليه
Fourth: From the connected specifiers is the adjective (sifah), such as: 'Honor the knowledgeable men' – so the ignorant are excluded. It was said: Its specification is not verbal, so on this basis it is not from the connected but from the independent, and it has been covered in the issue of whether the specified general is real or metaphorical. And the adjective, when it follows multiple items conjoined by 'waw' like Tamim and the tall Quraysh, is like the exception in following conjoined sentences in terms of position and preferred view. And know that specification by condition, limit, and adjective is only according to those who affirm the contradictory implication (mafhum mukhalafah); so it entails the non-establishment of the ruling for some (individuals). As for those who negate the implication, they do not say they specify. Thus it is in 'al-Tahrir'. I say: It is not so; rather, it appears that specification in the sense of restriction (qasr) is agreed upon between us and those who affirm the implication. The only difference is in affirming the opposite ruling for the excluded part: those who affirm the implication say yes, and those who negate say no. So reflect. And the truth is what the author of 'al-Tahrir' said, for the general in these forms is used in its meaning, and it is not restricted to some at all according to the Hanafis, as you have known: the conditional particle excludes both ends from completeness and conveys a conditional ruling in all individuals, but the result occurs when the condition is realized; if it is realized in some, then in some, otherwise in all, and if it is not realized at all, then not. And the limit particle conveys the termination of the general's ruling if it accompanies it; so it rules on the limited terminated by the limit, not that the general is used in it. And the adjective restricts the genus first, then its generality is considered in the individuals of the restricted by the wise name's designation, as in the idafah construct. Unlike the Shafi'is, for when they affirmed the implication, these restrictions became effective: they withdraw the ruling from some individuals of the general, so the general's ruling on them contradicts, and by this contradiction it is understood that the intent from it is the other individuals, as in the independent specifier. As for us, it is not so, because if the intent from the general were what contains the condition and adjective, the meaning would be 'Honor the knowledgeable men if they are knowledgeable' or 'Honor the knowledgeable men who are knowledgeable' – and you see that; rather, no meaning remains for the condition and other restrictions except emphasis, unlike their view. For their meaning for them is the opposite ruling for the unstated. Then, the Shafi'i school is hardly valid in any way. First, because if the intent from the general were the individuals that exist in the condition or adjective or the limited by the limit, repetition would be understood, and empirical evidence denies it. Second, because these restrictions are not independent; they convey meaning only after attachment to what precedes, and they are only suitable for attachment by way of emphasis. So the restrictions have a benefit other than negating the ruling, so the implication is not established due to the absence of what they have stipulated for its establishment. Understand and be steadfast. Then you have known that in exception too the general remains on its meaning, and when it is restricted by exclusion, a meaning is understood from the compound that applies to the remainder by the type-based designation (al-wadʿ al-nawʿī) of compounds. So it too is not specification. And the author of 'al-Tahrir' (may his secret be sanctified) omitted it because he chose what the (original) author chose: that the intent from the first part is the remainder, and the exception is a clue. So it has become clear that what the Shafi'is count as connected specifiers has no restriction (qasr) at all, and the truth is what he held.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about whether the adjective (sifah), together with condition (shart) and limit (ghayah), are truly connected specifiers (mukhassisat muttasila). The author first notes the rule: when an adjective follows multiple nouns conjoined by 'waw', it operates like exception in terms of returning to all or only the last. The preferred view is the Hanafi position. Then the core dispute: According to those who affirm the concept of 'mafhum al-mukhalafah' (the understanding that a restricted ruling implies the opposite for what is not mentioned), these restrictions function as specifiers. The Hanafis (who generally reject mafhum al-mukhalafah) do not consider them true specifiers. The author argues that specification in the sense of restriction (qasr) is agreed upon; the real difference is whether the opposite ruling (naqid) is established for the excluded part. He then explains the Hanafi view: in conditional sentences, the general word is used in its full meaning, and the condition only restricts the application temporally, not the individuals. Similarly, ghayah indicates the endpoint of the ruling, not reduced scope. Adjectives restrict the genus, not the general's application to all individuals. The Shafi'i view would lead to tautology (e.g., 'honor knowledgeable men who are knowledgeable'). Moreover, the Shafi'i understanding is linguistically unsound because restrictions cannot be both independent and attached. Finally, the author extends the argument to exception (istithna'), arguing that it too is not true specification but a type-based compound meaning. The truth is that none of these are specifiers in the strict sense; the general remains operative.

Key Terms
صفة (ṣifah): An adjective that qualifies a noun, considered a connected specifier by some, but the author argues it restricts the genus rather than specifying the general.
مفهوم المخالف (mafhūm al-mukhālafah): The understanding that a restricted ruling implies the opposite for what is not mentioned; Hanafis generally reject this.
قصر (qaṣr): Restriction of the general to some individuals; the author argues this is the real meaning of takhsis, and even Hanafis agree on this, but they differ on whether these restrictions achieve it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the function of an adjective like 'knowledgeable' in 'Honor the knowledgeable men'?
A) It specifies the general 'men' to exclude the ignorant
B) It restricts the genus 'men' to a subset, but the general remains applicable to all men
C) It serves as emphasis only
D) It has no effect

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that adjectives, conditions, and limits do not specify the general but restrict the genus; the genuine dispute is over whether they establish the opposite ruling for what is not mentioned.

Connected Qualifiers as Mukhassis: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Analyzing the Hanafi rejection of takhsis by connected qualifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on connected qualifiers as mukhassis and evaluate the Shafi'i argument based on mafhum al-mukhalafa.

Core Idea
Connected qualifiers do not specify the general; they only provide conditions or emphasis.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut, the author examines the fourth type of connected mukhassis: the attribute. He contrasts the Hanafi view, which denies that conditions, limits, and attributes function as specifiers, with the Shafi'i view, which relies on mafhum al-mukhalafa. The dispute is not about practical outcome but about the mechanism: Hanafis see qualifiers as merely descriptive or conditional, while Shafi'is treat them as indicators of exclusion. The author argues that the Shafi'i position leads to linguistic absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three connected qualifiers
The passage discusses attribute (sifah), condition (shart), and limit (ghaya) as potential connected mukhassis.
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis do not consider them true mukhassis; the general remains general and the qualifier provides emphasis or condition.
The general is used in its full meaning, and the qualifier restricts the genus, not the general.
3
State the Shafi'i position and proof
Shafi'is use mafhum al-mukhalafa: the qualifier indicates the ruling applies only to the described part, implying the opposite for others.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response argues that the Shafi'i view leads to redundancy (e.g., 'Honor the learned men if they are learned') and the qualifier has no independent specifying function.
The author emphasizes that the qualifier is only suitable for emphasis.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: for a conditional command, Hanafis apply the ruling to all individuals when the condition is met; Shafi'is restrict to those with the condition, implying the opposite for others.

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth type of connected mukhassis (al-sifah, attribute). The author begins by defining it: a descriptive clause that restricts the general. He then reviews a debate: some say sifah is not a verbal mukhassis (meaning it is not a separate part of speech that explicitly excludes), but rather an independent mukhassis in reality. The author clarifies that according to Hanafis, condition, limit, and attribute do not specify the general at all; rather, the general remains fully operative. The difference between Hanafis and Shafi'is is not about the existence of a contrary ruling, but about the mechanism. Shafi'is rely on mafhum al-mukhalafa: the qualifier indicates that the ruling applies only to the qualified part, implying the opposite for others. Hanafis reject this: the qualifier is mere emphasis or condition, not an indicator of exclusion. The author then provides a detailed critique of the Shafi'i position, arguing that it would lead to absurd redundancy and that the qualifiers have no independent meaning. He also includes exception (istithna) in this discussion, noting that even there the general is not specified; rather, the composite yields a new meaning. The passage concludes that only independent mukhassis (like separate verses or hadith) can specify the general.

Key Terms
مخصصات متصلة (mukhassisat muttasila): Connected specifiers – qualifiers that are part of the same utterance as the general, such as condition, limit, and attribute
صفة (sifah): An attribute or descriptive clause that qualifies the general term
مفهوم المخالف (mafhum al-mukhalafa): The contrary implication: the understanding that the ruling for the unmentioned is the opposite of that for the mentioned

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, connected qualifiers do not specify the general; they provide conditions or emphasis, and the general remains fully operative over the determined set.

Badal al-Ba'd: Is It a Connected Mukhassis?
Discussing whether substitution of part is a connected specifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether badal al-ba'd functions as a connected mukhassis according to Hanafi usul and explain the reasoning.

Core Idea
Badal al-ba'd does not specify the general; it only adds emphasis.

This passage examines a subtle issue in Hanafi usul: whether the substitution of a part (badal al-ba'd) restricts the general term. While some scholars treat it as a mukhassis, the Hanafi position is clear—it does not. Let's explore the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the construction
Recognize badal al-ba'd as a linguistic construction where a part replaces the whole in the ruling, e.g., 'أكرم بني تميم العلماء منهم' (Honor Banu Tamim, the learned among them).
The substitute (العلماء) is a part of the substituted-from (بني تميم).
2
Examine the claim of specification
Some scholars argue that the substitute is the sole intended referent, making the general term (al-mubdal minhu) effectively set aside—thus badal al-ba'd would be a mukhassis.
This view is held by many usul scholars outside the Hanafi school.
3
Counter-claim: substituted-from not disregarded
Verifiers like al-Zamakhshari maintain that the substituted-from is not entirely ignored; it serves as an introduction to the substitute, providing emphasis and clarification.
The generality of the substituted-from is still in force.
4
Understand the Hanafi rejection
Our teachers (Hanafi usul scholars) did not list badal al-ba'd as a mukhassis because the substituted-from retains its own meaning. If the part were truly intended alone, it would be substitution of the whole (badal al-kull), since the substitute must be identical in essence.
The function is to add emphasis (tawkid) and clarification (tabiin), not restriction.
5
Conclude the legal effect
Thus, badal al-ba'd is not a true mukhassis; the general remains unrestricted. The construction only emphasizes that the ruling applies particularly to the part, without excluding other individuals of the general category.
This aligns with the Hanafi principle that connected mukhassisat must actually restrict the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الخامس) من المخصصات المتصلة بدل البعض نحو أكرم بني تميم العلماء منهم، ولم يذكره الأكثرون من أهل الأصول (قيل) إنما لم يذكروه لأن المبدل منه في نية الطرح) لأن البدل هو المقصود بالنسبة فلا اعتداد فلا ولا يخص ( وفيه نظر لأن الذي عليه المحققون كالزمخشري ومثله في تحقيق كون البدل مقصوداً بالنسبة أن المبدل منه في غير بدل الغلط ليس في حكم المهدر ) مطلقاً حتى لا يعتبر عمومه وخصوصه (بل هو) جيء به (للتمهيد والتوطئة لذكر البدل ليفاد) بمجموعهما فضل تأكيد وتبيين لا يكون في الإفراد لأن النسبة متكررة (هذا) واعلم أن مشايخنا إنما لم يذكروه، لأن المبدل منه مستعمل في معناه، كيف لو أريد به البعض الذي هو البدل صار بدل الكل لأن المعتبر فيه عينية لما استعمل فيه المبدل منه وإنما نسب إلى الحكم لقصد توطئة النسبة إلى البدل ليفيد فضل توكيد، فليس هذا من المخصصات فتدبر
(The fifth) of the connected mukhassisat is substitution of part, like 'Honor the Banu Tamim, the learned among them.' Most usul scholars did not mention it. (It was said) they did not mention it because the substituted-from is intended to be set aside, since the substitute is the intended in relation to the ruling, so no attention is paid to it and it does not specify. But there is a consideration because what the verifiers like al-Zamakhshari and others hold in verifying that the substitute is intended in relation to the ruling is that the substituted-from, except in case of error, is not entirely disregarded, so that its generality and specificity are not considered. Rather, it is brought for introduction and preparation to mention the substitute, so that by the two together additional emphasis and clarification are achieved that would not be in a single expression because the relation is repeated. This. And know that our teachers only did not mention it because the substituted-from is used in its meaning. How? If the part which is the substitute were intended, it would become substitution of the whole, because what is considered in it is the same identity as what the substituted-from is used for, and it is only attributed to the ruling with the aim of preparing the relation for the substitute to provide additional emphasis. So this is not among the mukhassisat. Ponder.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage examines the fifth connected mukhassis: badal al-ba'd (substitution of part), e.g., 'Honor Banu Tamim, the learned among them.' The author notes that most usul scholars did not list this as a mukhassis. Some argue it is because the substituted-from (al-mubdal minhu) is mentally set aside, so only the substitute matters. However, the author counters that according to verifiers like al-Zamakhshari, the substituted-from is not entirely disregarded; it serves as an introduction to the substitute, providing emphasis and clarification. Hanafi teachers did not include it as a mukhassis because the substituted-from retains its own meaning. If the substitute (the part) were really the only intended, it would be badal al-kull (substitution of the whole), since the substitute must be the same entity as the substituted-from. The function of badal al-ba'd is to add emphasis, not to restrict. Therefore, it does not truly specify the general.

Key Terms
بدل البعض (badal al-ba'd): Substitution of part – a syntactic construction where a part replaces the whole in the ruling, but the whole remains the subject
مبدل منه (mubdal minhu): The substituted-from – the original term that is replaced by the substitute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they considered it a type of istithna.
B) Because the substituted-from is entirely disregarded.
C) Because the substituted-from retains its meaning and the substitute adds emphasis only.
D) Because the substitute must be identical to the substituted-from.

Key Takeaway
Badal al-ba'd is an emphasis tool, not a specifier; the general term remains operative in Hanafi usul.

Badal al-Ba'd: A Connected Specifier
Defining badal al-ba'd as a connected specifier and explaining why it is accepted or omitted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define badal al-ba'd as a connected specifier and explain the Hanafi position on its status, including the reasoning of different usul scholars.

Core Idea
Badal al-ba'd specifies the general by substituting a part, but the general remains operative as an introduction for emphasis.

This lesson explores the fifth type of connected specifier: badal al-ba'd. The passage defines it with the example 'Honor the Banu Tamim, the scholars among them' and discusses why many usul scholars omitted it from their lists. We will analyze the reasoning of the verifiers and the Hanafi mashayikh to understand the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of badal al-ba'd
Recognize that badal al-ba'd consists of a general term (mubdal minhu) followed by a specific part (badal) that replaces it in attribution. The part must be a subset of the general.
2
Understand the controversy
Note that many usul scholars omitted badal al-ba'd because they considered the general term as effectively omitted (fi niyyat al-tarh), so it does not require specification.
This is the view of 'aktharun min ahl al-usul'.
3
Analyze the verifiers' position
The verifiers (like al-Zamakhshari) argue that the general term is not disregarded; it serves as a preparation (tamhid wa tawtia) for the substitute, adding emphasis. Therefore, it is a specifier.
The passage uses 'fihi nazar' to indicate this is the preferred view.
4
Examine the Hanafi mashayikh's view
The Hanafi scholars (mashayikhuna) omitted it because in proper usage the general retains its meaning and the part is intended separately; thus it is not a specifier but a form of emphasis. However, the muhaqqiqun accept it as a mukhassis.
The passage ends with 'fatadabbar' encouraging reflection.
5
Apply the definition
Test with other examples to see if they qualify as badal al-ba'd. Ensure the substitute is a part of the general and the attribution is transferred.
Example: 'I ate the bread, half of it' - is it badal al-ba'd?

(الخامس) من المخصصات المتصلة بدل البعض نحو أكرم بني تميم العلماء منهم، ولم يذكره الأكثرون من أهل الأصول (قيل) إنما لم يذكروه لأن المبدل منه في نية الطرح) لأن البدل هو المقصود بالنسبة فلا اعتداد فلا ولا يخص ( وفيه نظر لأن الذي عليه المحققون كالزمخشري ومثله في تحقيق كون البدل مقصوداً بالنسبة أن المبدل منه في غير بدل الغلط ليس في حكم المهدر ) مطلقاً حتى لا يعتبر عمومه وخصوصه (بل هو) جيء به (للتمهيد والتوطئة لذكر البدل ليفاد) بمجموعهما فضل تأكيد وتبيين لا يكون في الإفراد لأن النسبة متكررة (هذا) واعلم أن مشايخنا إنما لم يذكروه، لأن المبدل منه مستعمل في معناه، كيف لو أريد به البعض الذي هو البدل صار بدل الكل لأن المعتبر فيه عينية لما استعمل فيه المبدل منه وإنما نسب إلى الحكم لقصد توطئة النسبة إلى البدل ليفيد فضل توكيد، فليس هذا من المخصصات فتدبر
(The fifth) of the connected specifiers is badal al-ba'd (substitution of a part), e.g., 'Honor the Banu Tamim, the scholars among them.' Most scholars of usul did not mention it. (It was said) They only did not mention it because the substance replaced (mubdal minhu) is intended to be omitted (fi niyyat al-tarh), because the substitute (badal) is the intended one in terms of attribution, so no consideration is given (to the general), so it does not specify. (But there is consideration) because the position of the verifiers like al-Zamakhshari and others in verifying that the substitute is intended in terms of attribution is that the substance replaced, apart from badal al-ghalat, is not in the ruling of being disregarded (muhdar) absolutely, such that its generality and specificity are not considered. Rather, it is brought for introduction and preparation (tamhid wa tawtia) for mentioning the substitute, so that by both together an extra emphasis and clarification is achieved that would not be in single mention because the attribution is repeated. (This is it) And know that our shaykhs (mashayikhuna) only did not mention it because the substance replaced is used in its meaning; how? If by it the part that is the substitute were intended, it would become badal al-kull (substitution of the whole) because what is considered in it is the essence of what the substance replaced is used for, but it is attributed to the ruling for the purpose of introducing the attribution to the substitute to achieve extra emphasis, so this is not among the specifiers, so ponder.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of connected specifier: badal al-ba'd, where the general term is followed by a specific part as a substitute. The author explains why many usul scholars omitted it from their lists: they considered the general term (mubdal minhu) as effectively omitted, so it does not need specification. However, the verifiers like al-Zamakhshari maintain that the general term is not disregarded; it is introduced to prepare for the substitute, adding emphasis. The Hanafi scholars (mashayikhuna) omitted it because in proper usage the general term retains its meaning and the part is intended separately, making it not a true specifier but a form of emphasis. The key point is that badal al-ba'd is a mukhassis according to the preferred view.

Key Terms
بدل البعض (badal al-ba'd): A substitution of a part where the general term is followed by a specific part that replaces it in attribution

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did some Hanafi scholars omit badal al-ba'd from the list of connected specifiers?
A) They considered it a type of disconnected specifier
B) They viewed the general term (mubdal minhu) as intended to be omitted, thus not requiring specification
C) They did not recognize any substitution in Arabic
D) They preferred badal al-kull instead

Key Takeaway
Badal al-ba'd is a valid connected specifier that restricts the general to a part, with the general serving as an introduction for emphasis.

Practical Custom as a Specifier (Al-Urf al-Amali)
Debating whether practical custom can specify a general term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts practical custom as a specifier and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Practical custom specifies a general term because it creates immediate understanding (tabadur), just like verbal custom.

In Hanafi usul, both verbal and practical custom can specify a general term. This lesson examines the debate with the Shafi'i school, focusing on the example of 'flesh' (lahm) and the underlying criterion of immediate understanding. You will see how induction (istiqra) supports the Hanafi position and why the Shafi'i distinction between absolute and general is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
State the question: Can practical custom ('urf amali) specify an 'amm (general) term? Note the disagreement: Hanafi says yes, Shafi'i says no.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the example 'Buy flesh' where custom determines the specific meat. Understand that immediate understanding (tabadur) of the specific meaning occurs due to custom. This is the same mechanism as in verbal custom.
The text uses ghalabat al-'ada (preponderance of custom) leading to ghalabat al-ism (preponderance of the noun).
3
Evaluate the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'is argue that specifying a general changes its meaning, unlike restricting an absolute. Understand their distinction between mutlaq and 'amm.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafi response rejects the distinction: the criterion (munat) is tabadur, which exists in both cases. The Hanafi also uses induction (istiqra) to show that custom creates immediate understanding, like grammatical rules.
The author calls the Shafi'i position 'clear arbitrary judgment' (tahakkum).
5
Conclude with the practical difference
In contracts, Hanafi interprets words according to prevailing custom, while Shafi'i requires explicit specification. This affects rulings in transactions and oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة العرف العملي) أي تعامل الناس ببعض أفراد العام (مخصص) للعام بتلك الأفراد (عندنا خلافاً للشافعية، كحرمت الطعام وعادتهم أكل البر (انصرف الطعام (إليه) عندنا خلافاً لهم (وأما) التخصيص بالعرف القولي بأن جرى العرف بهجران الاستغراق للكل، بل كلما أطلقوا في العرف أرادوا بعض الأفراد (فباتفاق) بيننا وبينهم مخصص كالدراهم) تطلق على النقد الغالب) في العقود لنا الاتفاق على فهم لحم الضأن بخصوصه في قوله : (اشتر لحماً، وقصر الأمر عليه حتى لو اشترى غيره لم يكن ممتثلاً إذا كانت العادة أكله وما ذلك إلا لتبادر الخصوص وهو متحقق في العملي كالقولي فيخصص هو مثل تخصيصه فالفرق بين المطلق المقيد والعام والمخصص كما في شرح المختصر » بأنه يجوز تقييد المطلق بالعرف العملي، ولا يجوز تخصيص العام لأنه في تقييد المطلق يبقى المطلق وفي تخصيص العام يتغير العام معناه، واشتر لحماً من القبيل الأول دون الثاني، فلا يصح الاستدلال به فإنه في غير محل النزاع (لغو) غير مسموع (إذ المناط) في تقييد المطلق بهذا (التبادر) إلى التقييد للتعامل وهو موجود في تخصيص العام قيل: هذا أي قياس العام على المطلق (قياس في اللغة) فلا يقبل (أقول) في دفعه ليس قياساً في اللغة بل استقراء فإن الاستقراء شهد بأن ما يوجب التبادر إلى غير الموضوع له يوجب إرادته تجوّزاً كرفع الفاعل ثبت باستقراء الفواعل الأخرى في الرفع (فتأمل) فإنه الحق الشافعية قالوا الصيغة المستعملة مع العرف العملي (عامة) لغة (ولا مخصص) فيبقى على عمومه (قلنا) المقدمة الثانية ممنوعة فإن عادتهم مخصصة لصيغتهم، لأن غلبة العادة ينجر إلى غلبة الاسم كالدراهم على) النقد (الغالب) فالباعث في العرف القولي الذي هو مخصص بالاتفاق ليس إلا غلبة العادة فإنه لا باعث للخصوص) فيه إلا أن استعماله أغلب) فالقول بتخصيص القولي وصيرورته قرينة دون العملي تحكم صريح لا يسمع، ومن ههنا ظهر وجه آخر للمدعي وهو اشتراك القولي والعملي في المناط، وبما قررنا اندفع أن غلبة العادة إذا انجر إلى غلبة الاسم صار المخصص عرفاً قولياً ولا نزاع فيه، مع أنه كلام على السند فتدبر
Issue: Practical custom ('urf amali) i.e., people's dealing with some individuals of the general (is a specifier) for the general by those individuals (according to us, contrary to the Shafi'is). For example, 'Flesh is prohibited' while their custom is to eat beef: 'flesh' is restricted (to it) according to us, contrary to them. (As for) specification by verbal custom (al-'urf al-qawli) such that custom runs with abandoning generality for the whole, rather whenever they utter in custom they intend some individuals, it is (by agreement) between us and them a specifier, like 'dirhams' which are applied to the dominant coin in contracts. For us, there is agreement on understanding lamb meat specifically in his saying: 'Buy flesh' and restricting the command to it such that if he bought other than it he would not be complying, if the habit was to eat it. And that is only due to the immediate understanding (tabadur) of specificity, and it is realized in practical custom like verbal custom, so it specifies just like its specification. The difference between absolute and restricted (mutlaq and muqayyad) and general and specific as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: it is permissible to restrict the absolute by practical custom, but not to specify the general, because in restricting the absolute the absolute remains, whereas in specifying the general the meaning of the general changes. The example 'Buy flesh' belongs to the first category not the second, so the argument by it is not valid; it is in a different context of dispute. (It is) nonsense, not acceptable, because the criterion (al-munat) in restricting the absolute by this is the immediate understanding of restriction due to dealing, which exists in specifying the general as well. It was said: this is a qiyas in language, hence not acceptable. (I say) in refuting it, it is not qiyas in language but induction (istiqra), for induction testifies that whatever necessitates immediate understanding to something other than the original meaning necessitates intending it metaphorically, just as the raising of the subject (raf' al-fa'il) is established by induction of other subjects in raising. (So reflect) for it is the truth. The Shafi'is said: The expression used with practical custom is general linguistically, and there is no specifier, so it remains upon its generality. (We said) The second premise is denied, for their custom is a specifier for their expression, because preponderance of custom leads to preponderance of the noun, like dirhams for the dominant coin. Thus the motive in verbal custom, which is a specifier by agreement, is nothing but the preponderance of custom; there is no motive for specificity except that its usage is more frequent. So the claim of specifying the verbal custom and making it a context (qarina) without the practical is clear arbitrary judgment not to be heard. From here another aspect for the claimant appears: the sharing of verbal and practical custom in the criterion. And by what we have established, it is repelled that if preponderance of custom leads to preponderance of the noun, the specifier becomes a verbal custom, and there is no dispute in it, though it is speech on the chain, so ponder.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position that practical custom ('urf amali) can specify a general term, contrary to the Shafi'is who only accept verbal custom ('urf qawli). The author gives the example of 'flesh' (lahm) being understood as the locally common meat due to custom. He refutes the Shafi'i argument that the distinction between absolute and general prevents such specification, showing that the same principle of immediate understanding (tabadur) applies in both. The Hanafi view based on induction (istiqra) and the equivalence of both types of custom in creating immediacy of meaning. The debate highlights the importance of 'urf as a source of specification in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
العرف العملي (al-urf al-amali): Practical custom; the dealing of people with some specific individuals of a general term, such that the term is understood as applying to those individuals
التبادر (al-tabadur): The immediate understanding of a specific meaning upon hearing a term, due to custom or habit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary criterion (munat) that the Hanafi school uses to justify specification by both verbal and practical custom?
A) The literal meaning of the word
B) The immediate understanding (tabadur) caused by custom
C) The agreement of the community
D) The explicit mention in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Practical custom (urf amali) is accepted by the Hanafi school as a specifier of general terms, based on the same criterion of immediate understanding (tabadur) that applies to verbal custom.

Can Practical Custom Specify General Texts? The Role of Ghalabah
Debate on whether customary practice can specify general texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that practical custom can specify general texts by analyzing the argument that predominance (ghalabah) converts it into verbal custom.

Core Idea
When custom dominates the name, it becomes a linguistic specifier—practical custom specifies via ghalabah.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses a key debate in Hanafi usul: can a widespread customary practice ('adah) serve as a specifier (mukhassis) for a general text? The author refutes the claim that only verbal custom ('urf qawli) can specify, arguing that when a practice becomes overwhelmingly dominant, it linguistically shapes the term itself, like 'dirhams' referring to the prevalent coin. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for interpreting general legislative statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
State the view that only verbal custom can specify general texts, not practical custom.
The opposing view distinguishes between 'urf qawli and 'urf 'amali.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how the author denies the second premise and argues that custom itself is a specifier through ghalabah.
The key phrase: 'غلبة العادة ينجر إلى غلبة الاسم'.
3
Examine the dirham example
See how the term 'dirham' shifted from general to specific due to prevailing usage.
The example illustrates how custom dominates the name.
4
Evaluate the common ground
Recognize that both verbal and practical custom share the same operative cause (manat): ghalabah.
This refutes the arbitrary distinction.
5
Reflect on the critique
Consider why rejecting practical custom as a specifier is described as 'تحكم صريح' (sheer arbitrary judgment).
The call to 'ponders' (فتدبر) invites deeper contemplation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخصص) فيبقى على عمومه (قلنا) المقدمة الثانية ممنوعة فإن عادتهم مخصصة لصيغتهم، لأن غلبة العادة ينجر إلى غلبة الاسم كالدراهم على) النقد (الغالب) فالباعث في العرف القولي الذي هو مخصص بالاتفاق ليس إلا غلبة العادة فإنه لا باعث للخصوص) فيه إلا أن استعماله أغلب) فالقول بتخصيص القولي وصيرورته قرينة دون العملي تحكم صريح لا يسمع، ومن ههنا ظهر وجه آخر للمدعي وهو اشتراك القولي والعملي في المناط، وبما قررنا اندفع أن غلبة العادة إذا انجر إلى غلبة الاسم صار المخصص عرفاً قولياً ولا نزاع فيه، مع أنه كلام على السند فتدبر.
Specifier) so it remains on its generality. (We said) the second premise is denied, for their custom is a specifier for their expression, because the predominance of custom leads to the predominance of the name, like dirhams for the prevalent coin. So the motivator in verbal custom, which is a specifier by agreement, is nothing but the predominance of custom, for there is no motivator for specificity in it except that its usage is more prevalent. So the claim that the verbal custom specifies and becomes a qarinah without the practical custom is sheer arbitrary judgment, not to be accepted. From here another point becomes clear for the claimant, namely the common ground between the verbal and the practical. And by what we have established, it is refuted that if predominance of custom leads to predominance of the name, then the specifier becomes a verbal custom, and there is no dispute about it. However, this is speech on the chain of transmission, so ponder.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on whether 'adah (customary practice) can serve as a mukhassis (specifier) for general legislative texts. The author refutes an opposing view that distinguishes between verbal custom (al-'urf al-qawli) and practical custom (al-'urf al-'amali). He argues that when a practice becomes overwhelmingly dominant (ghalabah), it leads to the linguistic predominance of the term, making it a verbal custom that specifies. Thus, the claim that only verbal custom can specify while practical custom cannot is arbitrary. The author uses the example of the word 'dirhams' which, due to common usage, came to refer specifically to the prevalent coin. The passage ends with a call to ponder (tadabbur).

Key Terms
العادة (al-‘ādah): Customary practice that becomes prevalent; when dominant, it can specify general linguistic expressions.
العرف القولي (al-‘urf al-qawlī): Verbal custom; the common understanding of a word in a particular community based on prevalent usage.
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): Predominance or overwhelming prevalence of a custom or usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It must be practiced by all Muslims without exception.
D) It must be approved by a consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
When custom dominates the name, it becomes a verbal custom that can specify general texts—a key Hanafi principle in the role of 'urf.
