بها نفي الحصة المحتملة مع صفة الوحدة، فلا ينافي تحققها مع حصة أخرى، فهذا ليس من العام وهو مراد شرح الشرح، ولهذا زاد قوله من غير استغراق، غاية ما في الباب أن هذا الاستعمال يكون مجازاً ولا ضير، لكن بقي ههنا شيء هو أن النهي عن الفرد المنتشر يوجب حرمة الإتيان بالجميع بل له الإتيان بالكل إلا الواحد، فالنهي عن المطلق من جهة الانتشار يستدعي الكف عن واحد من أفراده، ويتحقق الامتثال بإتيان المقيد، والكف عن واحد مما عداه ، والنهي عن المقيد يستدعي أن لا يأتي بواحد من أفراده إن كانت وأن لا يأتي به إن لم تكن له أفراد كثيرة، ففي الإتيان به أو أفراده مأثم، فحينئذ لا يمكن العمل بهما، فلا بد من الحمل أو النسخ كما في المثبتين، فلا بد من إرادة العموم فليس من هذا الباب، فالأولى أن يراد بالمطلق ما لا يكون فيه قيد، وإن كان عاماً وبالمقيد ما فيه قيد فلا يضر كونه عاماً والبحث وإن مر لكن كرر لزيادة الفائدة، ثم إنه لم يذكر بحث أفراد فرد من العام في كتب مشايخنا الكرام كأصول الإمام فخر الإسلام ونحوها، فالأحرى الحمل على ما قلنا، ويؤيده تمثيل اختلاف السبب بالإطلاق والتقييد أيضاً كما سيظهر فافهم
And the second case: that both [texts] are in one ruling with the same cause and both are negative, as indicated by his statement: (And if the ruling is unified) with the same cause, then if both are negative, they are both acted upon (by consensus, and one is not carried over to the other because there is no contradiction due to the possibility of acting upon both, as you say in zihar: 'Do not free a mukatab' and 'Do not free a disbelieving mukatab') because it is possible to act by refraining from both. And in Sharh al-Mukhtasar, this is from the (general) because the indefinite under negation is from the absolute, so it is from another chapter, namely specifying an individual from the general. (And it is known) from before that it is not specified except by the understanding of the adjective. So accordingly, it should be specified according to the Shafi'is with consensus, so understand. (And in Sharh al-Sharh, this is a criticism of the example, and this is not the habit of scholars. (And) the correct example: 'Do not free the mukatab' without (comprehensiveness but a mentally prevalent [meaning]) as in 'Buy the meat.' I say in response that the purpose of the objection is that the negation of the probable portion which is the meaning of the absolute is general, like the indefinite. So every indefinite or mentally prevalent [noun] that falls under negation becomes a negation of the probable portion in them. So it is not a criticism of the example but of the thing exemplified. And what he mentioned of the example is that the mentally prevalent, like the indefinite, is general under negation, so it is also from the chapter of the general. And this is insufficient, for you have known that the reality of the negated indefinite, even if it is negation of all individuals, sometimes is used for negation of a single unit as well, like: 'No man came, but two men' and the author has stated earlier that the negated indefinite other than with the generic la is not a text for generality, so it is possible that it is intended for the negation of the probable portion with the quality of unity, so it does not contradict its realization with another portion. So this is not from the general, and this is the intention of Sharh al-Sharh. For this reason he added his statement 'without comprehensiveness.' The most that can be said is that this usage is a metaphor and no harm. But here remains something: that the prohibition of the distributed individual necessitates the prohibition of bringing all, but rather he may bring all except one. So the prohibition of the absolute from the perspective of distribution requires refraining from one of its individuals, and compliance is achieved by bringing the restricted, and refraining from one other than it. And the prohibition of the restricted requires that he not bring one of its individuals if it has [many], and that he not bring it if it does not have many individuals. Then in bringing it or its individuals there is sin. So then it is not possible to act upon both, so it is necessary to carry over or abrogate as in the positive ones, so it is necessary to intend generality, so it is not from this chapter. So it is better to mean by the absolute what has no restriction, even if it is general, and by the restricted what has a restriction, so it does not harm if it is general. And the discussion even if passed, it is repeated for added benefit. Then, he did not mention the discussion of specifying an individual from the general in the books of our great masters like Uṣūl al-Imām Fakhr al-Islām and similar works, so it is more appropriate to carry over as we said. And it is supported by the example of difference of cause with absoluteness and restriction as will appear, so understand.

Commentary
This passage explains the second case where an absolute and a restricted command share the same ruling and cause and are both negative (e.g., prohibitions). The principle is that they can both be applied without contradiction because it is possible to refrain from both prohibited acts. The text then enters a detailed discussion about the nature of the negated indefinite (al-nakira al-manfiyya). The author critiques an example from Sharh al-Sharh, arguing that the negated indefinite, though normally general, can also be used to negate a single unit. They conclude that the proper understanding is that the absolute here means what has no restriction, even if general, and the restricted has a restriction. The discussion touches on the Hanafi view of 'am (general) and the rule when both texts are prohibitions.

Key Terms
النكرة المنفية (al-nakira al-manfiyya): A negated indefinite noun, which in this discussion is debated as to whether it indicates generality or a specific instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when both an absolute and a restricted prohibition exist on the same act (like the zihar example), the Hanafi ruling is:
A) The restricted overrides the absolute, so the absolute becomes inoperative.
B) Both are applied by refraining from both prohibited acts.
C) The absolute overrides the restricted, so the restricted is redundant.
D) Neither is applicable because they contradict.

Key Takeaway
When absolute and restricted prohibitions share the same cause, the Hanafi school applies both without conflict, and the negated indefinite is not automatically general – a key distinction from other schools.

Is the Word 'Amr' Shared or Common? The Hanafi Position on Ishtirak vs Tawatu'
Arguments for and against lexical sharing in 'amr'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the word 'amr' (command) regarding whether it is ishtirak (shared term) or tawatu' (common meaning), and apply the reasoning to distinguish between the two concepts in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school rejects both pure ishtirak and tawatu for 'amr', adopting ishtirak ma'nawi: the word primarily denotes the speech, and the act is metaphorical.

This passage presents a three-way debate on the linguistic nature of the word 'amr'. The Hanafi position navigates between two extremes: the claim that 'amr' has two completely separate meanings (ishtirak lafzi) and the claim that it has a single abstract meaning covering both speech and act (tawatu'). Understanding this debate is crucial for properly interpreting commands in the revealed sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Conflicting Views
Read the passage and list the positions of the proponents of ishtirak and the proponents of tawatu'. Note the core claims each group makes about the word 'amr'.
Proponents of ishtirak rely on the principle of literalness (al-asl al-haqiqah) to argue that both meanings are literal. Proponents of tawatu' argue for a single common meaning to avoid metaphor and ishtirak.
2
Locate the Hanafi Argument
Identify the Hanafi commentator's objections to both positions. The text states that tawatu' is an innovation (qawl hadith) and that the word was historically literal only for speech.
The Hanafi response uses the historical precedence of the literal meaning to reject tawatu'.
3
Analyze the Key Reasoning
Understand why the commentator rejects pure ishtirak and tawatu' and prefers ishtirak ma'nawi. The argument involves the principle that literalness is the default, but when the word is extended to a related meaning (the act), it becomes metaphorical, leading to a meaning-based sharing.
The passage also mentions Hanafis and Hanbalis accepting metaphorical plurals, showing that metaphorical extension is accepted.
4
Recognize the Preferred View
Identify that the commentator settles on ishtirak ma'nawi (meaning-based sharing) as the preferred Hanafi view. This avoids the indeterminacy of pure ishtirak and the overgeneralization of tawatu'.
Ishtirak ma'nawi means the word has a primary literal meaning (speech) and a secondary metaphorical meaning (act) derived from a common conceptual link.
5
Apply to a New Text
Consider how this debate affects usul al-fiqh practice. For example, when encountering the word 'amr' in a Quranic verse or hadith, a Hanafi mujtahid will interpret it as primarily denoting the imperative speech, and the commanded action as an implied meaning.
This distinction prevents confusion in deriving obligations (wujub) from commands.

المفرد لا يوجب التجوز في الجمع فإذن لا نسلم لزوم الاختراع، وبهذا يندفع الإيراد الأول أيضاً، لأن ندرة الجمع باعتبار المعنى المجازي المغاير له باعتبار الحقيقي ممنوع كيف وقاعدة التغليب في التثنية والجمع مشهورة كيف وقد ذهب إليه الحنابلة والحنفية مع أنهم من العرفاء بلسان العرب العرباء، وقد حكموا على جموع المذكر السالم بأنه جمع للمفرد باعتبار المعنى المجازي، وبان لك أيضاً أن ما ذكره لا يصلح للمعارضة، نعم : لو قرر المنع بأن الاشتراك لا يوجب أن لا يحمل عليه إذا دل القرينة وثبت من أهل اللغة أنه حقيقة فيهما لم مجمع استعلاء لما مر عدم أصالة الكف حتماً يبعد فتدبر، قائلو الاشتراك قالوا أطلق) لفظ الأمر (لهما) أي القول والفعل (والأصل الحقيقة) فيحمل عليها، وهو الاشتراك (وقد تقدم جوابه) وهو أن الأصل عدم الاشتراك، ولك أن تقرر بأنه أطلق لهما على السواء، وإلا لما تعرضت له كتب اللغة والأصل الحقيقة دفعاً للترجيح من غير مرجح، وحينئذ لا يتوجه الجواب، قائلو التواطؤ (قالوا) كل من القول والفعل أمران اشتركا في عام فيجعل اللفظ المستعمل فيهما له دفعاً للاشتراك والمجاز) لأنهما خلاف الأصل (قلنا) القول بالتواطؤ (قول حادث، فإن كونه حقيقة في القول المخصوص بخصوصه (عليه قبل ظهور هذا القول فلا تردد حينئذ في كونه للقول (حتى يترجح) الاشتراك (المعنوي) فإن العمل بالأصالة عند التردد لا غير فافهم
The singular does not necessitate figurativeness in the plural, so we do not accept the necessity of invention. And with this, the first objection is also repelled, because the rarity of the plural considering the metaphorical meaning different from the literal meaning is denied. How so, when the rule of predominance in duals and plurals is well-known. Indeed, the Hanbalis and Hanafis have held this despite being experts in the pure Arabic tongue, and they ruled that sound masculine plurals are plurals of the singular considering the metaphorical meaning. And it also became clear to you that what he mentioned is not suitable for opposition. Yes, if the prevention is established that sharing does not necessitate that it not be applied to it when the context indicates and it is confirmed from linguists that it is a literal meaning in both, it would not be far. So reflect. The proponents of ishtirak said: 'The word amr is applied to both (i.e., speech and act), and the principle is literalness, so it is applied to both, and that is ishtirak.' And its answer has preceded: 'The principle is the absence of ishtirak.' But you may establish that it is applied to both equally, otherwise language books would not have mentioned it, and the principle of literalness avoids preference without a preferred, and then the answer does not apply. The proponents of tawatu said: 'Both speech and act share a common [meaning], so the word used for them is made for that common meaning, to avoid ishtirak and metaphor because they are contrary to the principle.' We said: 'The claim of tawatu is an innovation. For its being literal for the specific speech in particular existed before the emergence of this claim, so there is no hesitation then in it being for speech such that the meaningful ishtirak is preferred. For acting upon the principle [of literalness] occurs only in cases of hesitation, so understand.'
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh concerning whether the Arabic word 'amr' (command/amr) is used for both speech (the utterance 'do') and the action (the commanded act) as a shared term (ishtirak) or whether it has a single common meaning that applies to both (tawatu). The text first discusses an argument about plural forms: the opponent claimed that using the plural for metaphorical meanings is rare, but the author responds that Hanafis and Hanbalis accept the metaphorical use in sound masculine plurals, so it is not rare. Then the proponents of ishtirak argue that the word 'amr' is applied to both speech and act equally, and the principle of literalness demands we accept it as a shared term. The author notes that the earlier response—that the principle is against ishtirak—is insufficient because if the word is truly used for both equally, then literalness would force us to accept ishtirak. The proponents of tawatu argue that both speech and act share a common property, so the word is made for that common meaning, avoiding ishtirak and metaphor. The author rejects this, stating that before this innovation, the word was well-established as literal only for the specific speech (the imperative form), so there is no hesitation; only in hesitation does one need to appeal to the default meaning. Therefore, the preferred position is ishtirak ma'nawi (meaning-based sharing), not tawatu.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirak): Linguistic sharing; a word used for two or more distinct meanings.
تواطؤ (tawatu'): A word used for multiple things due to a common meaning they share.
اشتراك معنوي (ishtirak ma'nawi): Meaning-based sharing; a word with a primary literal meaning that extends metaphorically to related meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on the word 'amr' according to this passage?
A) It is purely shared (ishtirak lafzi) between speech and act.
B) It is a tawatu term with a single common meaning.
C) It originally refers only to speech, and its use for action is metaphorical, leading to ishtirak ma'nawi.
D) It is a homonym with no connection between speech and act.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school avoids both pure ishtirak and tawatu for the word 'amr', adopting ishtirak ma'nawi, where the word literally refers to the speech but metaphorically extends to the act, preserving the original literalness while allowing contextual application.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Imperative Form Indicates Obligation: Hanafi Proofs and Counter-Arguments
Arguments proving imperative form indicates obligation by default in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the imperative form indicates obligation by default, and evaluate the evidence from the Quran and Salaf against Shafi'i objections.

Core Idea
The imperative form is placed for obligation; any deviation requires a contextual indication.

This lesson examines the foundational Hanafi claim that the imperative form (e.g., افعل) in its default sense imposes a binding command (وجوب). We will trace the four main proofs from the Quran and the practice of the Salaf, and respond to the Shafi'i position that the form is shared among obligation, recommendation, and permission. By the end, you will be able to derive rulings from imperative texts and critically assess alternative interpretations.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi thesis and the opposing view
Begin by identifying the exact point of dispute: does the imperative form (صيغة الأمر) default to obligation, or is it shared among obligation, recommendation, and permission? The Hanafi school holds the former; the Shafi'i school and others hold the latter.
This sets the frame for all subsequent proofs.
2
Examine the Salaf consensus argument
Analyze the claim that the Companions and Followers consistently used imperative forms for obligation, and their silent consensus (إجماع سكوتي) proves the default meaning. Consider the objection that this inference is merely suppositional (ظني) and the Hanafi response that it yields certain knowledge through repeated observation.
Focus on the epistemic weight of the consensus: it is treated as a source of certainty in linguistic matters.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof from Surah al-A'raf 7:12
Examine the rebuke of Iblis for not prostrating when commanded. The rebuke only makes sense if the command was for obligation. Discuss the rebuttal that an unstated contextual indication may have existed, and why the Hanafi author considers such a possibility remote and non-detrimental to the apparent meaning.
This verse is a primary textual proof for the default obligation.
4
Analyze the Quranic proofs from Surah al-Mursalat 77:48 and al-Nur 24:63
Verse 77:48 blames those who do not bow when told, directly linking blame to the command form. Verse 24:63 threatens punishment for opposing the command, which would be unjustified if the command were non-obligatory. Follow the istinbat chain that derives obligation from the threat of punishment.
Note how these verses work together to reinforce the default reading.
5
Consider the additional proof from Surah Taha 20:93 and the concept of disobedience
The passage concludes with the argument that leaving a command is termed 'disobedience' (عصيان), which only applies to obligatory commands. This confirms the default obligation through the linguistic usage of the term.
This proof ties together the lexical and scriptural arguments.

(رابعاً) قوله تعالى : ( فَلْيَحْذَرِ الَّذِينَ يُخَالِفُونَ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ أَن تُصِيبَهُمْ فِتْنَةٌ أَوْ يُصِيبَهُمْ عَذَابٌ أَلِيرُ ﴾ [النور: (٦٣] ) (والـمـراد) مـنـه (إيـجـاب الحذر إذ لا معنى للندب ههنا فإن الفعل إن كان تركه موجباً للعذاب فالحذر لازم، وإلا فلا وفي هذه الزيادة دفع لما قيل إن الدليل متوقف على أن يكون هذا الأمر للوجوب وهو ممنوع وإن بني على أن الأمر للوجوب دار وجه الدفع إثبات كونه للوجوب من غير بناء وضعه للوجوب أولاً، فإن الأمر بالحذر لا يصلح للندب، وغيره سوى الوجوب (وهو) أي وجوب الحذر (دليل الوجوب إذ لا حذر في مخالفة غير الواجب، فإن قلت: ندب أيضاً، على أن هي، هي
أن يجوز أن يكون المراد بالمخالفة حمله على غير المراد وهي حرام سواء كان الحكم ندباً أو وجوباً، أو يكون المراد عدم اعتقاد الحقية، وهي حرام أيضاً في كل حكم من الأحكام قال : وحمل المخالفة على حمله على ما يخالفه (مراداً أي حمله على مخالفة المراد بالأمر بأن يحمل على غيره ما يكون مراده تعالى ( أو ) حمل المخالفة (عليها اعتقاداً) بأن يعتقد خلاف ما حكم تعالى به بعيد فإن المتبادر من خالف أمره ترك المأمور (به والحمل على المحمل البعيد لا يكون إلا لصارف، وإذ ليس فليس ( إن قيل أمره (مطلق فلا يلزم منه كون كل أمر للوجوب (قلنا) ما هو مطلق (بل) عام لإضافة المصدر) وهي تفيد العموم وصحة الاستثناء) فإنه يصح يقال : خالف أمره إلا هذا الأمر وهذا غير وافٍ فإنه لا شك في أن بعض صيغ الأمر نادبة ومبيحة، فلا يمكن دعوى العموم ههنا وما أورد من الدليلين إنما يفيد أن وضع لفظ أمره للعموم، لا أن المراد ههنا العموم، ودفع هذا الإيراد بأن غاية ما لزم منه أن العام مخصوص بما لا يدل القرينة على أنه لغير الوجوب والعام المخصوص حجة في الباقي، ورده الشيخ الهداد بأنه للخصم أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون مخصوصاً بما يدل القرينة على الإيجاب، وفيه أن هذا تخصيص من غير مخصص بخلاف ما دل القرينة على غير الوجوب، فإن الإجماع دل على أنه ليس للوجوب، والإجماع مخصص قطعاً، وقد أجيب عن أصل الإيراد بأنه إن كان مطلقاً يفيد المدعي أيضاً، لأن ترتب الوعيد على مخالفته ينادي على الوجوب قال في «الحاشية». وفيه ما فيه، ووجه بأنه على تقدير الإطلاق يكون في قوة الجزئية، لأن المهملة في قوتها فلا يفيد المطلوب، وفيه غفلة عن تحرير الجواب، فإن حاصله أن ههنا مصدراً مضافاً، وإذا لم تكن للاستغراق فهي للجنس ويتبادر من الآية وجوب الحذر لمخالفة جنس الأمر فيكون وضعه للعموم، وإلا لما صلح هذه المخالفة علة لوجوب الحذر، وبه اندفع أيضاً ما أورد أنه يجوز أن يكون الذين يخالفون مفعول فليحذر وفيه ضمير الفاعل الراجع إلى الفسقة، والمعنى: فليحذر الفسقة عن أنفسهم كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْتُلُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ ﴾ [البقرة: ٥٤] وذلك لأنه على هذا أيضاً يتبادر أن السبب هو مخالفة الأمر وفيه المدعي وأيضاً هذا بعيد غاية البعد لا يجوزه العرف قطعاً، ثم ههنا إشكال أورده مطلع الأسرار الإلهية» قدس سره أنه قد أن حقيقة مر الأمر اقتضاء فعل حتماً فحينئذ معنى الآية فليحذر الذين يخالفون طلبه الحتمي وحينئذ صح العموم ولا يلزم منه كون صيغة الأمر للوجوب، بل يجوز أن تكون الصيغة حقيقة في الندب ، فلا تكون هذه الصيغ أوامر فلا يترتب على مخالفته الوعيد نعم يتم هذا الاستدلال على إبطال ما يقول الشافعية إن المندوب مأمور به ويمكن دفعه بما قرر الشيخ الهداد أن الكريمة دلت على أن مخالفة الأمر موجبة للوعيد وترك المندوب والمباح لا يوجب الوعيد بوجه فالأمر ليس إلا الطلب الحتمي، وقد أجمع على أن صيغة افعل أمر فهي للوجوب فتأمل فيه تأملاً صادقاً واستدل أولاً بأن تارك المأمور به عاص (بدلیل قوله تعالى حكاية عن موسى أَفَعَصَيْتَ أَمْرِى [طه: ۹۳] مخاطباً لأخيه هارون (أي أخلفني في قومي قاله حين أراد الذهاب إلى الطور لأخذ التوراة كما قص الله سبحانه في كتابه وكل عاص متوعد بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَعْصِ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ
The third article on linguistic principles. Among the (shared among three) of obligation, recommendation, and permission, and attributed to the Rawafid (and it was said) it is a form placed for the common meaning among them, which is permission whether accompanied by prohibition from leaving or not, and attributed to al-Murtada al-Rafidi as well. The (Shia) said according to what they chose: it is shared among four (the three mentioned and threat). So those schools are (ten complete, and may be increased and decreased). For us, firstly: the inference of the Salaf (the noble Companions and the good Followers) with it for obligation, and this inference became widespread and public without any denial. So that indicated their consensus that it is for it. For if it is said: perhaps that inference (the mentioned one) was with contextual indications of obligation, so it does not indicate the lexical setting, as evidenced by their inference with many of them for recommendation. We say: their inference was not by contextual indications, but by apparent meaning and immediate understanding, as evidenced by their turning to recommendation due to contextual indications without obligation by correct induction. And an objection that it is a supposition in principles (usul) so not considered because it is a silent consensus, which gives only supposition, and also the consensus is ahad (isolated) so does not give certainty. The answer: we do not accept that the silent consensus is suppositional; rather, it is ordinary knowledge that they agreed that the immediate understanding from them is obligation, achieved by repetition in observing their inferences with commands. And this is necessary knowledge that no doubt can approach at all, like experiential and observational knowledge. And its transmission is not ahad but mutawatir in meaning, for in every generation their inferences were transmitted in such a way as to give certainty that the inference was by immediate understanding. (And even if) it is granted that it is supposition, it is supposition in language, and supposition suffices in it, for most investigations of language are suppositional. (And even if it is granted that it is supposition in principles (usul),) this supposition suffices, otherwise acting upon most apparent meanings would be impossible because that is what is possible. For if you say: then how are the definite obligations established? I say: by the addition of other contextual indications indicating that these commands are for obligation definitively, so ponder. (And) for us (secondly) His statement: "What prevented you from prostrating when I commanded you?" [al-A'raf: 12] and the word 'not' (la) is added to emphasize prohibition. (And what is meant) by the command is "prostrate" (usjudu) stripped of any contextual indication. (And were it not for the mentioned command (for obligation) only, the rebuke would not be directed, for there is no rebuke in leaving what is not obligatory nor in leaving what is probable other than obligation. For if you say: it is possible that "prostrate" was preserved with a contextual indication of obligation but the Quran did not relate it, so it does not indicate the claim. He said: (The probability of a situational or verbal contextual indication that the Quran did not relate is not harmful to the apparent meaning.) For it is a remote probability not arising from evidence, so it is not considered, thus it does not detract from the apparent meaning. (And) for us (thirdly) His statement: "And when it is said to them, bow, they do not bow" [al-Mursalat: 48] for the purpose is blame for leaving bowing, and it is based on violating the form as such, so it indicates obligation, because its violation necessitates blame, and there is no sharing (of meanings), otherwise excuse would be permissible that it was not obligatory so blame would not follow from violating the form. (And) for us (fourthly) His statement: "So let those who oppose his command beware lest a trial strike them or a painful punishment" [al-Nur: 63] and what is meant by it is making caution obligatory, for there is no meaning of recommendation here. For if the act's leaving is productive of punishment, then caution is necessary, otherwise not. And in this addition is a refutation of what was said: that the evidence depends on this command being for obligation, which is denied; and if it is based on that the command is for obligation, the turn of the refutation is proving it to be for obligation without first building on its being placed for obligation. For the command to caution is not suitable for recommendation, and other than it besides obligation. (And it) i.e., the obligation of caution, (is evidence of obligation, for there is no caution in violating what is not obligatory). For if you say: also recommendation? Even so, that... it is possible that what is meant by opposition is interpreting it to something other than what is intended, which is prohibited whether the ruling is recommendation or obligation; or it means not believing the truth, which is also prohibited in every ruling. He said: And interpreting opposition as interpreting it contrary to what is intended, i.e., interpreting it to something other than the intended meaning of the command, or opposition in belief, i.e., believing contrary to what Allah has ruled, is far-fetched. For the immediate understanding of "opposed his command" is leaving the commanded thing. And interpreting it in a far-fetched manner is only done due to a countervailing factor, and since there is none, it is not. If it is said: His command is absolute, so it does not entail that every command is for obligation. We say: it is not absolute, but general due to the addition of the verbal noun (idafat al-masdar), which gives generality and validity of exception. For it is valid to say: he opposed his command except this command. But this is not sufficient, for there is no doubt that some imperative forms are recommendatory and permissive, so claiming generality here is impossible. And what is brought from the two proofs only benefits that the placement of the word 'amruhu' is for generality, not that what is intended here is generality. And the refutation of this objection is that the utmost that follows from it is that the general is specified by what does not indicate that it is for other than obligation, and the general when specified is a proof for the remainder. And Shaykh al-Haddad refuted it by saying: the opponent can say: it is possible that it is specified by what indicates obligation. In it is that this is specification without a specifying factor, unlike what indicates other than obligation, for consensus indicates that it is not for obligation, and consensus is a definite specifying factor. And it has been answered regarding the original objection that if it is absolute, it also benefits the claim, because the threat of punishment for opposing it proclaims obligation. He said in al-Hashiya: "In it is what is in it." And it was explained that on the assumption of absoluteness it would be in the strength of a particular, because the unspecified (muhmala) is in its strength, so it does not benefit what is sought. In it is heedlessness about refining the answer. For its essence is that here there is a verbal noun in the construct state, and if it is not for universal inclusiveness (istighraq), then it is for the genus. And from the verse, the obligation of caution for opposing the genus of command is immediately understood, so its placement is for generality. Otherwise, this opposition would not be suitable as a cause for the obligation of caution. And with this is also refuted what was objected: that it is possible that "those who oppose" is the object of "let them beware", and in it is a pronoun of the subject referring to the sinners. The meaning: let the sinners beware of themselves, as in His statement: "then kill yourselves" [al-Baqara: 54]. That is because on this also, the immediate understanding is that the cause is opposition to the command, and in it is what is claimed. And also this is extremely far-fetched, which custom absolutely does not allow. Then there is an objection here raised by 'Mutla' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya', may his secret be sanctified: that the reality of the meaning of command is demanding the action decisively. So then the meaning of the verse is: let those who oppose His decisive demand beware. And then the generality is sound, and it does not entail that the imperative form is for obligation; rather, it is possible that the form is real for recommendation, so these forms are not commands, so punishment does not follow from opposing it. Yes, this proof is complete for refuting what the Shafi'is say that the recommended (mandub) is commanded. And it can be refuted by what Shaykh al-Haddad established: that the noble verse indicated that opposition to the command necessitates punishment, and leaving the recommended and permissible does not necessitate punishment in any way. So the command is nothing but the decisive demand. And it has been agreed that the form 'if'al' is a command, so it is for obligation. So reflect upon it sincerely. And he also inferred firstly that the one who leaves the commanded thing is a disobeyer (asi), based on His statement narrated from Musa: "Did you then disobey my command?" [Taha: 93] addressing his brother Harun (i.e., did you go against my order to my people? He said that when he wanted to go to the Mount to receive the Torah as Allah narrated in His Book. And every disobeyer is threatened by His statement: "And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger..."
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi defense that the imperative form (صيغة الأمر) in its default usage indicates obligation (وجوب), not a shared meaning across obligation, recommendation, and permission as some schools claim. The author presents four main arguments:

1. **Salaf Practice**: The Companions and Followers consistently used imperative forms to indicate obligation, and this practice was widespread without any rejection, which constitutes a silent consensus (إجماع سكوتي) that the form is for obligation. The author argues that this consensus is not merely suppositional but yields certain knowledge through repeated observation. Even if it were supposition, it suffices in linguistic matters.

2. **Quranic Verse (al-A'raf 7:12)**: Allah rebuked Iblis for not prostrating when commanded. The rebuke would only be appropriate if the command 'prostrate' was for obligation; if it were merely recommended or permissible, rebuke would not follow. The possibility of an unstated contextual indication is rejected as remote.

3. **Quranic Verse (al-Mursalat 77:48)**: Those who are told to bow but do not bow are blamed. The blame is directly linked to violating the command form, proving that the form imposes obligation. If it had multiple meanings, one could excuse themselves by saying the command was not for obligation.

4. **Quranic Verse (al-Nur 24:63)**: The threat of punishment for opposing Allah's command shows that the command must be for obligation, as there is no threat for leaving a non-obligatory act. The discussion emphasizes that 'the command' (أمره) in the verse is general and applies to every command, and the threat is for leaving what is commanded, not for misinterpretation.

The text also responds to objections, particularly the Shafi'i view that the recommended (مندوب) is also commanded (مأمور به), using the verse to argue that since leaving a recommended act does not bring punishment, the command in the verse must be for obligation. It ends by citing Musa's rebuke of Harun (Taha 20:93) to show that leaving a command is disobedience (عصيان), which confirms obligation.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (sighat al-amr): The imperative form (e.g., افعل) which is argued to be placed for obligation by default.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Obligation: a command whose leaving is punishable.
الندب (al-nadb): Recommendation: a command whose leaving is not punishable but whose doing is rewarded.
القرينة (al-qarina): Contextual indication that turns the imperative from its default (obligation) to another meaning.
الإجماع السكوتي (al-ijma' al-sukuti): Silent consensus: when some scholars rule and others remain silent, indicating agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four main proofs used by Hanafi usul scholars to argue that the imperative form indicates obligation?
A) The silent consensus (إجماع سكوتي) of the Salaf
B) The rebuke of Iblis in Surah al-A'raf 7:12
C) The threat of punishment in Surah al-Nur 24:63
D) The use of the imperative in secular Arabic literature

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that the imperative form defaults to obligation is supported by the practice of the Salaf and the logic of Quranic rebuke and threat, making it a cornerstone for deriving rulings from commands.

Deriving Wujub from Quran 24:63: The Generality of the Construct Form
Derivation of wujub from Quran 24:63 using idafa and umum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the Hanafi rule that the command form (sigha if'al) indicates obligation (wujub) by analyzing Quran 24:63 using the principles of umum and idafa.

Core Idea
A command connected to a threat proves obligation; the construct form makes it general, so opposing any command incurs threat.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut demonstrates how the Hanafi school derives the fundamental usul principle that al-amr yufidu al-wujub (the command form indicates obligation) from Quran 24:63. The verse threatens those who oppose the Prophet's command, and the author analyzes the word 'amrihi' as a masdar mudaf (construct form) to establish its generality. By examining various objections and responses, you will see how the Hanafi proof is constructed and defended against competing interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key verse and operative phrase
Focus on the words 'yukhalifuna an amrihi' (oppose his command) in Quran 24:63. The verse attaches a threat (falyahdhar) to this opposition.
The threat is central to the argument.
2
Analyze the linguistic form of 'amrihi'
The word 'amrihi' is a masdar mudaf (construct form). In Hanafi usul, the idafa (construct) gives generality (umum) – it covers all commands, not just a specific one.
This is a key Hanafi claim; other schools may disagree on the generality of idafa.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle linking generality and threat
Since opposition to any command incurs threat, the command must be a decisive demand (talab jazim). Recommended acts (nadb) do not incur threat for leaving them.
This step logically connects the threat to obligation.
4
Address the objection that some commands are not obligatory
The author acknowledges that some command forms (e.g., for nadb or ibaha) are not obligatory. He responds by explaining that the general is specified (takhsis) by evidence, but the remaining commands still prove obligation.
Takhsis does not invalidate the generality; it narrows it.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab reading
The Shafi'i school holds that 'amr' can be for nadb or wujub depending on context. The Hanafi response shows that the threat in the verse proves wujub as a default, and consensus (ijma) on the form if'al being a command supports obligation.
Understanding this dispute is crucial for usul al-fiqh.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الإيراد بأن غاية ما لزم منه أن العام مخصوص بما لا يدل القرينة على أنه لغير الوجوب والعام المخصوص حجة في الباقي، ورده الشيخ الهداد بأنه للخصم أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون مخصوصاً بما يدل القرينة على الإيجاب، وفيه أن هذا تخصيص من غير مخصص بخلاف ما دل القرينة على غير الوجوب، فإن الإجماع دل على أنه ليس للوجوب، والإجماع مخصص قطعاً، وقد أجيب عن أصل الإيراد بأنه إن كان مطلقاً يفيد المدعي أيضاً، لأن ترتب الوعيد مضافاً، وإذا لم تكن للاستغراق فهي للجنس ويتبادر من الآية وجوب الحذر لمخالفة جنس الأمر فيكون وضعه للعموم، وإلا لما صلح هذه المخالفة علة لوجوب الحذر، وبه اندفع أيضاً ما أورد أنه يجوز أن يكون الذين يخالفون مفعول فليحذر وفيه ضمير الفاعل الراجع إلى الفسقة، والمعنى: فليحذر الفسقة عن أنفسهم كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْتُلُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ ﴾ [البقرة: ٥٤] وذلك لأنه على هذا أيضاً يتبادر أن السبب هو مخالفة الأمر وفيه المدعي وأيضاً هذا بعيد غاية البعد لا يجوزه العرف قطعاً، ثم ههنا إشكال أورده مطلع الأسرار الإلهية» قدس سره أنه قد أن حقيقة مر الأمر اقتضاء فعل حتماً فحينئذ معنى الآية فليحذر الذين يخالفون طلبه الحتمي وحينئذ صح العموم ولا يلزم منه كون صيغة الأمر للوجوب، بل يجوز أن تكون الصيغة حقيقة في الندب ، فلا تكون هذه الصيغ أوامر فلا يترتب على مخالفته الوعيد نعم يتم هذا الاستدلال على إبطال ما يقول It is permissible that what is meant by opposition is to carry it to other than what is intended, and it is forbidden whether the ruling is recommended or obligatory, or what is meant is not believing the truth, and it is also forbidden in every ruling. He said: Carrying opposition to carrying it contrary to what is intended means carrying it contrary to the intended meaning of the command, or carrying opposition to believing it, i.e. believing contrary to what Allah has ruled, is far-fetched, because what is immediately understood from "opposed his command" is leaving the commanded thing, and carrying it to a far-fetched interpretation only occurs due to a contravening indication, and since there is none, there is none. If it is said: His command is absolute, so it does not entail that every command is for obligation. Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and proof discussion: the author is establishing that the word 'amr' (command) in the Quranic verse 'فليحذر الذين يخالفون عن أمره' (Quran 24:63) indicates obligation. The passage begins by analyzing the meaning of 'mukhalafa' (opposition). It first defines two possible interpretations: (1) acting contrary to the intended meaning of the command (even if the ruling is recommended), which is forbidden, and (2) believing contrary to Allah's ruling, which is also forbidden. The preferred interpretation is that 'opposing His command' means leaving the commanded act, as this is the immediate understanding. Far-fetched interpretations require a countervailing indication, which is absent. The author then addresses an objection: that 'amri' (His command) is absolute and does not necessarily entail that every command is for obligation. He responds by arguing that 'amri' is not absolute but general due to the construct form (idafat al-masdar), which gives generality and allows exception (e.g., 'He opposed His command except this command'). However, the objector may say that some command forms are for recommendation or permission, so the claim of generality fails. The author concedes the point about some forms being non-obligatory, but he presents a rebuttal: the general can be specified by evidence indicating non-obligation, and the remaining part (the unspecified commands) remains a proof for obligation. Sheikh al-Haddad then refines this: the opponent could say that the general is specified by evidence indicating obligation, but this would be specification without a specifier. In contrast, specifying by evidence for non-obligation is valid because consensus (ijma') is a definite specifier. The author then offers a more fundamental answer: even if 'amri' is absolute, the very threat of punishment on opposition indicates that the command is for obligation—otherwise, the threat would apply to recommended acts, which is false. He criticizes the objection that an absolute statement is like a particular and does not prove the point, arguing that the source word in construct form is either for totality or for genus, and the verse's immediate implication is that opposing the genus of command necessitates caution, thus proving that 'amr' is for obligation. He also addresses an alternative reading of the verse's syntax (making 'alladhina yukhalifun' the object) and shows it is far-fetched. Finally, he presents an objection by Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya: the reality of 'amr' is to demand action decisively, so the verse is about opposing a decisive demand, but the command form itself could be literal for recommendation (i.e., it is a demand, but not necessarily for obligation). However, the author counters that the verse threatens those who oppose the command, and recommending an act does not entail threat for leaving it. The command must be a decisive demand. Moreover, there is consensus that the command form (if'al) indicates obligation. Thus, the argument stands against the Shafi'i view that the recommended is 'commanded' (ma'mur bih).

Key Terms
المخالفة (al-mukhalafa): Opposition to a command, interpreted here as either acting contrary to the intended meaning or disbelieving the ruling. The immediate understanding is leaving the commanded act.
الأمر (al-amr): A command; in usul, it is a demand to do an act. The author argues it is for wujub (obligation) by default.
العموم (al-umum): Generality; the quality of a word covering all individuals. Here, 'amri' is considered general due to idafa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The threat on opposition shows the command is a decisive demand.

D) The verse applies only to the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes that al-amr yufidu al-wujub by appealing to the generality of the construct form and the threat in Quran 24:63, with the understanding that this default can be specified by evidence.

The Imperative Form: Obligation or Shared Meaning? A Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Refuting Shafi'i arguments and defending Hanafi ishtirak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Shafi'i arguments regarding the default meaning of the imperative form and explain why the Hanafi school holds it is shared (ishtirak).

Core Idea
The imperative form alone does not indicate obligation; it is shared among obligation, recommendation, and permission until a qarina specifies.

In this passage, the author refutes the Shafi'i claim that the imperative form (sigha al-amr) defaults to obligation (wujub). Drawing on Qur'anic verses, hadith, and linguistic principles, the Hanafi position is defended: the imperative is shared among multiple meanings and requires contextual indicators (qara'in) to determine its legal force. This dispute has profound implications for interpreting revealed commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central dispute
Understand that the issue is whether the imperative form indicates obligation by default or is shared among multiple meanings (ishtirak). The Hanafi school argues for ishtirak; the Shafi'i school argues for wujub.
2
Examine the first Shafi'i proof and Hanafi rebuttal
The Shafi'is cite Qur'an 72:23 ('Whoever disobeys Allah...') to argue that anyone who abandons a commanded act is threatened, implying obligation. The Hanafi response shows this would make mandub (recommended) acts also threatened, which is contradictory. Thus the imperative cannot always indicate wujub.
The minor premise is general and cannot be restricted to only those commands without qarina.
3
Analyze the ishtirak argument
The Shafi'is claim ishtirak is contrary to the principle (khilaf al-asl) and that the imperative must be for one meaning. The Hanafis distinguish verbal ishtirak from semantic ishtirak and argue that semantic ishtirak is not inherently problematic. Moreover, specificity (al-khusus) is not always preferable to generality.
The text also discusses the difference between 'give me a drink' and 'I recommend you to give me a drink' to show that the imperative form can carry blame, indicating a different meaning.
4
Evaluate the hadith and linguistic arguments
The Shafi'is use the hadith 'When I command you with a matter, bring from it what you can' to argue that the command is left to our will. The Hanafis respond that it refers to capability, not will, and that obligation is conditional on ability. The linguistic argument that command and request differ only in rank is refuted: the rank of the commander is precisely what makes the imperative for wujub.
The understanding of the Companions of the Prophet's commands as obligatory is decisive.
5
Conclude the tarjih (preference)
The text clearly advocates the Hanafi position, emphasizing that it is the truth (fa-hafa huwa al-haqq). The Hanafi view best explains the data and avoids the contradictions of the Shafi'i position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
فإن 
فَإِنَّ لِمَنْ عَصَى اللَّهَ نَارَ جَهَنَّمَ ﴾ [الجن: ۲۳] فتارك المأمور به متوعد فيكون الأمر للوجوب (وفي «التحرير» إضافة أمر عهدي ولا نسلم تجرده أي تجرد الأمر عن القرينة وهي أن إجلاس النبي الخليفة كان لإنفاذ أحكام الله تعالى فيكون واجباً على الخليفة قبوله فما قيل إن الأمر ههنا مجرد عن القرينة ساقط وهذا سهل لقوله تعالى في حق الملائكة لَا يَعْصُونَ اللَّهَ مَا أَمَرَهُمْ ﴾ [التحريم: ٦] فلنا أن نغير الاستدلال ونثبت الصغرى بهذه الآية (وقد يمنع استلزام دليل الكبرى) إياها، وهي كل عاص متوعد مستنداً بأن المراد) بمن يعص الله (الكفار) بقرينة الدوام) والتأبيد وحمله على المكث الطويل بعيد كل البعد لا يستطيع المستدل ادعاءه والأولى) في إثبات الكبرى (التحويل على الاتفاق) فإن الإجماع منعقد على أن العاصي متوعد، هذا : ويرد عليه ما مر غاية ما لزم أن العصيان بمخالفة الأمر لا بمخالفة الصيغة، ويدفع بضم الإجماع على أن الصيغة أمر فتذكر (أقول : بهذا الدليل تمسك الشافعية في كتبهم على أن صيغة الأمر للوجوب (وقد غفلوا أنه ينفي ما ادعوه من كون المندوب مأموراً (به فإنه لو كان مأموراً به كان تاركاً عاصياً بالدليل المذكور بعينه وكل عاص متوعد كما ذكر، فتارك المندوب متوعد هذا خلف (وهل هذا إلا تناقض إلا أن يراد تارك المأمور به بصيغة افعل مجردة عن القرائن عاص، والحاصل التقييد في الصغرى وفيه ما فيه فإن دليل إثبات الصغرى عام فلا يصح التقييد، وفيه أنه لا تنافي بين كون المندوب مأموراً به وبين كون الصيغة للوجوب، فإن معنى الأول أن المندوب تعلق به صيغة الأمر، ومعنى الثاني هذه الصيغة للوجوب، ولا شبهة في عدم المنافاة بينهما . وحاصل الاستدلال أن تارك ما وقع عليه صيغة أفعل حقيقة عاص وكل عاص متوعد، وهذا غير واف، فإنه ليس الغرض أن بين مفهومي الدعويين تنافياً، بل إن هذا الاستدلال ينفي كون المندوب مأموراً به لأن الصغرى كلية هي أن كل تارك مأمور به عاص، وإرادة ما وقع عليه الصيغة حقيقة لا يزيد على ما ذكره المصنف والدليل العام ينفيه وإذا كان هذا الدليل ينفيه فادعاؤه مع اعتراف مقدماته في قوة التناقض فافهم واستقم (و) استدل (ثانياً للاشتراك خلاف الأصل فلا يكون مشتركاً بين اثنين أو أزيد فيكون لواحد من المعاني المذكورة (وغير الندب والوجوب من المعاني بعيد للقطع بفهم الترجيح في الفعل المأمور به، فيكون لأحدهما وانتفاء الندب للفرق بين اسقني وندبتك أن تسقيني) ولو كان للندب لم يكن بينهما فرق (فإنه يذم على الأول في الترك دون الثاني فعلم أنه فرق بينهما وفيه أن الخصم) وهو القائل بالندب (لا يسلم الفرق بينهما مطلقاً، بل يقول مفهوماً هما واحد من كل الوجوه (ولو سلم) الفرق (فبكونه) أي ندبتك أن تسقيني (نصاً) في الندب غير محتمل للوجوب (وعدمه) أي عدم كون اسقني نصاً فيه فإنه يحتمل انصرافه عنه بصارف (قيل وأيضاً لا ينفي الدليل الاشتراك المعنوي، فإنه ليس خلاف الأصل فإن أردت أن الاشتراك مطلقاً خلاف الأصل فممنوع وإن أردت أن الاشتراك اللفظي خلاف الأصل فيبقى شق المعنوي وفي «التحرير» لو قال المستدل الاشتراك المعنوي بالنسبة إلى معنوي أخص خلاف الأصل، إذ الخصوص أدخل في الإفادة) فهو أولى (اتجه) الدليل فإن مطلق الترجيح والإذن أعم من خصوص الوجوب، والكل معنى مشترك، فيكون الأخير أولى وفيه ما فيه لأن أرجحية الأخص ممنوعة، كيف وهي تستلزم أن يكون الإطلاق في مباينة الداخل تحت الأعم مجازاً، وهو خلاف الأصل بالنسبة إلى الحقيقة، وهذا ظاهر جداً، ولا ينفع كون الخصوص أدخل، فإن العموم ربما كان أحوط وأشمل فافهم أقول ما ذكره المستدل لنفي (الندب من حديث الفرق بين المذكورين (لو تم لدل على نفي المعنوي أيضاً إذ لا ذم في المرجح المطلق ويذم في أسقني ( فتدبر) قائلو الندب (قالوا أولاً) قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم إذا أمرتكم بأمر فأتوا منه ما استطعتم) رواه الشيخان (رده) رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم إلى مشيئتنا) والوجوب ينافيه، وبعد تسليم المقدمات التقريب غير تام لاحتمال أن يكون للإباحة، والمباح أيضاً مردود إلى المشيئة، إلا أن يقال إنه منتف بالضرورة لفهم الترجيح فتدبر فيه (قلنا) لم يرده إلى مشيئتنا (بل) رده (إلى استطاعتنا) وهو شأن الواجب فإن التكليف على حسب القدرة، كيف ولو كان المراد بالاستطاعة المشيئة، فسد المعنى فإن الأمر كما سبق الطلب الحتمي، فحينئذ يكون المعنى، إذا طلبت منكم طلباً حتمياً فأتوا منه ما شئتم ، ثم إنه لو سلم فلا يتم التقريب، فإنه لا يلزم منه أن الصيغة للندب، وإنما يلزم أن الطلب الحتمي للندب فتدبر (و) قالوا (ثانياً) نقل (عن أهل اللغة) العربية (لا فرق بين السؤال والأمر إلا بالرتبة فقط فإن الثاني من المستعلي، والأول من الأدنى وليس بينهما فرق في المعنى والسؤال للندب فكذا الأمر) له (أقول) في الجواب (الوجوب فرع الرتبة فإنه إنما يكون ممن له ولاية الإلزام والافتراق بالرتبة هو الموجب لكونه للوجوب وأما أنه ليس بينهما فرق في المعنى فممنوع كيف والصيغة موضوعة للوجوب، فيجب أن يصدر له ولاية الإيجاب، ولا يصح استعمالها للأدنى إلا تجوزاً وصرفاً عن الحقيقة، وأما النقل عن أهل العربية بأن الموضوع له واحد فيهما فيطالب تصحيحه ولو سلم فلا يعارض قولهم ما تواتر عن الصحابة والتابعين رضوان الله عليهم فإن قلت : النقل ثابت، فإن كتب الصرف والنحو مشحونة به، وأما انفهام الصحابة فلم يثبت إلا في أوامر الله تعالى وأوامر الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم ويجوز أن تكون الصيغة موضوعة لمعنى أعم فإذا صدر عن الأعلى الذي له ولاية الإلزام يفهم الوجوب لكون الرتبة قرينة عليه، فهو إذن حقيقة عرفية يفهم في عرف من له ولاية الإلزام، قلت انفهام الصحابة والتابعين مقطوع في أوامر الله تعالى ورسوله صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه، وأوضاع الصيغ ليست مختلفة بالنظر إلى المتكلمين، وكون من له ولاية الإلزام متكلماً لا يصلح قرينة الإيجاب، ويصح منه الندب ،والإباحة، وأما كونها حقيقة عرفية فقد مر في العام ما يفي ،لدفعه كيف ولم يثبت نص من الواضع أني وضعت هذا اللفظ لهذا المعنى، بل إنما يعلم من التبادر، وإذ قد جوزتم احتمال الحقيقة العرفية عند التبادر من غير قرينة انسد باب العلم بالوضع، وأما كتب النحو فلا يظهر منها أن الأمر في الدعاء، والإيجاب مستعمل فيما وضع له وليس فيها إلا بيان الصيغ ولو حسبوا ذلك فأي حجة في حسبانهم كما عرفت فاحفظه فإنه هو الحق ولا نسلم أن السؤال للندب بل لمطلق الطلب متضرعاً فلا يلزم من عدم افتراق الدعاء والأمر إلا بالرتبة كونه للندب
The third article on linguistic principles. Because 'Indeed, for whoever disobeys Allah is the fire of Hell' [Qur'an 72:23], so the abandoner of the commanded thing is threatened, thus the command is for obligation. (In al-Tahrir, there is addition of a command of covenant, and we do not accept its being free of the qarina, that the seating of the Prophet the Caliph was for executing Allah's rulings, so it is obligatory on the Caliph to accept it. So what is said that the command here is free of the qarina is invalid. This is easy because of His saying regarding the angels: 'They do not disobey Allah what He commands them' [Qur'an 66:6]. So we can change the inference and establish the minor premise with this verse. And it may be denied that the proof of the major premise necessitates it, that every disobedient is threatened, based on that what is meant by 'whoever disobeys Allah' is the disbelievers, by the indication of continuity and perpetuity, and interpreting it as long stay is extremely far, the inferrer cannot claim it. And the better way in establishing the major premise is to turn to consensus, for there is consensus that the disobedient is threatened. This: and there is responded to it what passed, the utmost that follows is that disobedience is by opposing the command, not by opposing the form. And it is repelled by adding consensus that the form is a command, so remember. (I say: With this proof the Shafi'is clung in their books that the imperative form is for obligation, and they overlooked that it negates what they claimed of the mandub being commanded, because if it were commanded, its abandoner would be disobedient by the same proof, and every disobedient is threatened as mentioned, so the abandoner of the mandub is threatened, this is a contradiction. Is this not a contradiction unless what is meant is that the abandoner of the commanded thing with the imperative form free of qarains is disobedient? The result is a restriction in the minor premise, and therein lies what it does. For the proof of establishing the minor premise is general, so restriction is not valid. And there is no incompatibility between the mandub being commanded and the form being for obligation, because the meaning of the first is that the mandub is attached to the imperative form, and the meaning of the second is that this form is for obligation, and there is no doubt of no incompatibility between them. The gist of the inference is that the abandoner of what the imperative form actually occurs upon is disobedient and every disobedient is threatened, and this is insufficient. For the purpose is not that there is incompatibility between the concepts of the two claims, but rather this inference negates the mandub being commanded because the minor premise is universal: every abandoner of the commanded thing is disobedient, and intending what the form actually occurs upon does not add to what the author mentioned, and the general proof negates it. If this proof negates it, then claiming it while acknowledging its premises is in the strength of contradiction, so understand and be upright. (And) he argued (secondly) that (ishtirak is contrary to the principle, so the form is not shared between two or more, so it is for one of the mentioned meanings. And other than nadb and wujub from among the meanings are far, because of the certainty of understanding preference in the commanded action, so it is for one of them, and the negation of nadb due to the difference between 'give me a drink' and 'I recommend you to give me a drink'. If it were for nadb, there would be no difference between them. For the first is blamed for omission while the second is not, so it is known that there is a difference between them. Therein that the opponent, who says nadb, does not concede the difference between them absolutely, but says the concepts are identical in all respects. (And if the difference is conceded, then because the one (i.e. 'I recommend you to give me a drink') is a nass in nadb, not probable for obligation, and the other (i.e. 'give me a drink') is not a nass in it, for it may be diverted from it by a diverting factor. (It was said: Also, the proof does not negate the semantic ishtirak, for it is not contrary to the principle. If you mean ishtirak absolutely is contrary to the principle, it is denied. If you mean verbal ishtirak is contrary to the principle, then the semantic aspect remains. In al-Tahrir: If the inferrer said: Semantic ishtirak with respect to a more specific meaning is contrary to the principle, because specificity is more conducive to clarity, then it is more appropriate. (The proof would be sound) for absolute preference and permission are more general than specific obligation, and all share a meaning, so the latter is more appropriate. Therein what it contains, because the preferability of the specific is denied. How can it be, if it necessitates that the absolute in being different from that which falls under the general is metaphorical, and that is contrary to the principle with respect to the literal? This is very obvious, and it does not benefit that specificity is more conducive, for generality may be more careful and comprehensive. So understand. I say: What the inferrer mentioned to negate nadb from the hadith of the difference between the two mentioned, if it were complete, would indicate the negation of the semantic also, for there is no blame in absolute preference, and there is blame in 'give me a drink', so reflect. (Those who say nadb said first): The Prophet (peace be upon him and his family and Companions) said: 'When I command you with a matter, bring from it what you can' (narrated by the two Shaykhs). (He referred it) the Prophet (peace be upon him and his family and Companions) to our will, and obligation contradicts it. After conceding the premises, the approximation is incomplete because it may be for permissibility, and the permissible is also referred to the will, unless it is said that it is negated necessarily due to understanding preference, so reflect on it. (We said) he did not refer it to our will, but rather he referred it to our capability, which is the matter of obligation, for the taklif is according to ability. How so? If the intention with capability were will, the meaning would be corrupted, for the command is as precedes: a categorical demand. Then the meaning would be: when I demand from you a categorical demand, then bring from it what you want. Then, if it is conceded, the approximation is not complete, for it does not entail that the form is for nadb, but rather it entails that the categorical demand is for nadb, so reflect. (And) they said (secondly) a transmission from the Arabic linguists: 'There is no difference between request and command except in rank only, for the second is from the superior and the first from the inferior, and there is no difference between them in meaning. Request is for nadb, so likewise command is for it.' (I say) in response: 'Obligation is a branch of rank, for it only comes from one who has authority of enforcement, and differentiation in rank is what necessitates it being for obligation. As for there being no difference between them in meaning, it is denied. How? The form is placed for obligation, so it must be issued by one who has authority of obligation, and its use by the inferior is not valid except metaphorically as a turn away from reality. As for the transmission from the Arab linguists that the thing placed is one in both, its verification is required. If conceded, it does not oppose their statement of that which is transmitted from the Companions and Followers, may Allah be pleased with them. If you say: The transmission is established, for the books of morphology and grammar are filled with it. As for the understanding of the Companions, it is not established except in the commands of Allah and the commands of the Prophet (peace be upon him and his family and Companions). And it is possible that the form is placed for a more general meaning, so when it comes from the superior who has authority of enforcement, obligation is understood because the rank is a qarina for it. So it is a conventional reality understood in the custom of the one having authority of enforcement. I say: The understanding of the Companions and Followers is decisive in the commands of Allah and His Messenger (peace be upon him and his family and Companions), and the placements of forms are not different with respect to speakers. And the fact that one who has authority of enforcement is a speaker does not serve as a qarina for obligation, and nadb and permissibility are valid from him. As for its being a conventional reality, what was mentioned regarding the general suffices to rebut it. How? And there is no established text from the positor that 'I placed this word for this meaning', but rather it is known from immediate understanding. And if you allow the possibility of conventional reality upon immediate understanding without a qarina, the door of knowledge of placement is closed. As for the books of grammar, it does not appear from them that the command in supplication and obligation is used for what it was placed for; there is only exposition of forms. If they considered that, then what authority is there in their consideration? As you have known, so preserve it, for it is the truth. And we do not concede that request is for nadb, but rather for absolute demand with humility, so it does not follow from the non-differentiation of supplication and command except in rank that it is for nadb.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute over the meaning of the imperative form (sigha al-amr). The Hanafi position, defended here, is that the imperative form alone is not automatically for obligation (wujub); it may be shared (ishtirak) among multiple meanings including obligation, recommendation (nadb), and permissibility (ibaha). The text begins by critiquing the Shafi'i proof that the imperative indicates obligation, using the Qur'anic verse that those who disobey are threatened with Hell. The author argues that this proof actually contradicts the Shafi'i claim that mandub (recommended acts) are commanded, because if mandub were commanded, its abandoner would be disobedient and threatened, which is absurd. Then the author examines the argument for ishtirak being against the principle (khilaf al-asl); he distinguishes between verbal ishtirak (lafzi) and semantic ishtirak (ma'nawi), and notes that semantic ishtirak is not necessarily contrary to principle. The discussion includes the difference between 'give me a drink' (imperative) and 'I recommend you to give me a drink' (explicit recommendation); the former carries blame if not performed, the latter does not, indicating a difference. The opponents (Shafi'is) also bring a hadith: 'When I command you with a matter, bring from it what you can.' They claim this refers to our will, but the Hanafis respond that it refers to capability, not will, and that obligation is conditional on ability. Finally, the linguistic argument that the only difference between request and command is rank is refuted; the Hanafi view is that the special rank of the commander (having authority) is precisely what necessitates obligation, not a mere difference in word meaning. The author stresses that the understanding of the Companions of the Prophet's commands as obligatory is decisive, not mere linguistic reports.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (sīghat al-amr): The imperative verbal form (e.g., if'al) that is the subject of dispute over its default legal meaning.
الوجوب (al-wujūb): Obligation; the legal quality of an act such that its performance is demanded and its omission is blameworthy and punishable.
الندب (al-nadb): Recommendation; an act whose performance is meritorious but omission is not blameworthy.
الاشتراك (al-ishtirāk): A single word having more than one meaning; here, the imperative form being shared between obligation, recommendation, and permission.
القرينة (al-qarīna): A contextual indicator that specifies which meaning of a shared term is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, what does the imperative form (sigha al-amr) indicate when no qarina (contextual indicator) is present?
A) It always indicates obligation (wujub).
B) It is shared (ishtirak) among meanings such as obligation, recommendation, and permission.
C) It always indicates recommendation (nadb).
D) It has no meaning until a qarina is added.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the imperative form is shared (ishtirak) and requires a qarina for obligation, a position that resolves the apparent contradiction of commanded yet non-obligatory acts.

Refuting the Common Meaning of the Imperative
Refuting the common-meaning claim for amr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the claim that amr has a common meaning between obligation and recommendation and explain the Hanafi response using the concept of inductive evidence.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that the imperative indicates obligation alone; the additional element is established by earlier proofs, so the common-meaning claim fails.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key objection to the Hanafi position that the imperative form exclusively indicates obligation. Opponents argue that by induction we only derive preference or permission, so obligation requires separate proof. The Hanafi response is that the additional element of obligation has already been proven by independent evidence, rendering the objection moot.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
What do the proponents of common meaning say about the imperative? They assert that the imperative has a shared meaning across obligation, recommendation, and permission, and that by induction we only understand preference (rajah) or permission (idhn), not the specific additional elements.
2
Understand the inductive basis
The opponents use inductive reasoning (istiqra') from linguistic usage to argue that no additional element like blame for leaving (for wujub) or specific preference (for nadb) is directly derived without extra proof.
Induction is a valid method in Hanafi usul, but its conclusions can be overridden by stronger evidence.
3
Locate the Hanafi response
The commentator refutes by stating that the additional element (obligation) has already been established by earlier proofs. Therefore, the opponents' claim that no proof exists is false.
This response relies on prior arguments in the text, which are not reproduced here.
4
Relate to previous proofs
The Hanafi school holds that the principle 'al-amr li al-wujub' (the imperative is for obligation) is proven by scriptural and rational evidence. These proofs are independent of the induction-based objection.
The passage refers to 'our previous proofs' as the basis for claiming the additional element is established.
5
Evaluate the strength of the refutation
The refutation works because it directly denies the opponent's premise (lack of proof) by citing existing evidence. It also avoids the objection that one is proving language by a necessary concomitant (lazim al-mahiya) by clarifying that the understanding of preference/permission comes from induction while obligation comes from separate evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قائلو الاشتراك المعنوي بين الندب والوجوب وبينهما والإباحة (قالوا : ثبت الرجحان أو الإذن بالضرورة الاستقرائية أي قال الأولون ثبت ،الرجحان وقال الآخرون : ثبت الإذن (فلم يثبت الزائد من الحرج في الترك على الرجحان، أو لم يثبت الزائد هو الرجحان لعدم الدليل) عليه فلا مدلول إلا (ذلك وإذا قرر كلامهم هكذا وأعرض عن التقرير المشهور بأن الرجحان أو الإذن لازم لما استعمل فيه الصيغة وخصوص الوجوب من غير دليل فلم يثبت أقول فاندفع ما في «المختصر وغيره أن فيه إثبات اللغة بلازم الماهية وهو ممنوع عنه، وجه الدفع أن المقصود أن فهم الرجحان أو الإذن بالاستقراء والزائد من غير دليل (فافهم قلنا) قد ثبتت الزيادة) على الرجحان أو الإذن بأدلتنا المتقدمة فعدم الدليل على الزيادة (ممنوع)
Those who say there is a common meaning between recommendation and obligation and between them and permission said: Preference or permission is established by inductive necessity. i.e., the first group said preference is established, and the others said permission is established. So the additional element of blame in leaving on preference, or the additional element of preference, is not established due to lack of proof. So there is no meaning except that. And when their speech is formulated like this and one turns away from the famous formulation that preference or permission is necessary for what the form is used for, and specifically obligation without proof, so it is not established. I say: So what is in al-Mukhtasar and others is repelled that it contains establishing a language by a necessary concomitant of the essence, which is denied. The repelling point is that the intended meaning is that understanding preference or permission is by induction, and the additional element is without proof. (So understand. We said:) The additional element (obligation) has been established by our previous proofs, so the claim of no proof for the additional element is denied.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of those who say the imperative has a common meaning between wujub and nadb, or between those and ibaha. They claim that by induction, we only understand either preference (rajah) or permission (idhn) from the command, but the additional element of blame for omission (on top of preference) or preference itself is not established without proof. Therefore, the only common meaning is that minimal understanding. The commentator refutes this by saying the Hanafi proofs have already established the additional element of obligation, so the claim of no proof is false. This refutes the objection that one cannot prove language by a necessary concomitant of the essence.

Key Terms
الاشتراك المعنوي (al-ishtirak al-ma'nawi): A single term used for multiple close meanings (like a genus that covers species)
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Inductive reasoning from linguistic usage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Hanafi reject the claim that the additional element (wujub) is not established?
A) Because the additional element is impossible
B) Because the additional element has been proved by earlier evidence
C) Because the common meaning is necessary
D) Because induction shows only permission

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school refutes the common-meaning theory of amr by pointing to independent proofs establishing obligation, rendering the induction-based objection invalid.

Rebuttal of the Skeptics on Knowledge of the Imperative
Refuting the claim that the imperative's meaning cannot be known

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the skeptics' argument against knowing the imperative's meaning and apply the Hanafi rebuttal using the concepts of tawatur, istiqra', and relative tawatur.

Core Idea
The imperative's default meaning of obligation is knowable through widespread inductive evidence, not through reason alone.

The skeptics (المتوقفون) argue that we cannot know whether the imperative form indicates obligation, permission, or recommendation because reason has no role in coinage and transmission is either ahad (not certain) or mutawatir (allegedly non-existent due to disagreement). The Hanafi response defends the knowability of the imperative's meaning through three lines: the role of reason as an auxiliary, the sufficiency of inductive conjecture (zann) in linguistic matters, and the reality of relative tawatur among those deeply familiar with Arabic usage. This lesson unpacks this critical debate in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the skeptics' position
Identify the claim that knowledge of the imperative's coinage is impossible because neither reason nor transmission yields certainty.
The skeptics deny both tawatur (due to later disagreement) and ahad (gives only zann, not 'ilm).
2
Locate the Hanafi responses
Find the three Hanafi counter-arguments: (1) reason has some entry, (2) zann from istiqra' suffices, (3) tawatur exists in early usage and can be relative.
The text explicitly labels these 'أولاً', 'ثانياً', 'ثالثاً'.
3
Analyze the concept of relative tawatur
Understand that tawatur may be specific to a group of specialists (e.g., linguists and jurists) and not extend to all people, explaining why some scholars disagreed later.
Relative tawatur is a distinctive Hanafi move to preserve epistemological certainty while acknowledging diversity.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Recognize the practical consequence: the Hanafi position that commands (awamir) are presumed obligatory unless proven otherwise, while the skeptics would suspend judgment.
This affects how mujtahids derive rulings from primary texts.
